"""Custom CUDA top-k kernel optimized for Blackwell SM120.

Two-pass design with pre-allocated temp buffers to avoid allocation overhead.
"""
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

CPP_SOURCE = r"""
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <vector>

std::vector<torch::Tensor> topk_forward_cuda(torch::Tensor x, int k,
    int num_blocks_per_row,
    torch::Tensor tmp_a_vals, torch::Tensor tmp_a_idxs,
    torch::Tensor tmp_b_vals, torch::Tensor tmp_b_idxs);
"""

CUDA_SOURCE = r"""
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <float.h>

__global__ void topk1_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x,
                             float* __restrict__ out_vals,
                             int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs,
                             int n) {
    int row = blockIdx.x;
    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    const float* row_x = x + row * n;

    float max_val = -FLT_MAX;
    int   max_idx = -1;

    int n4 = n >> 2;
    const float4* x4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_x);
    for (int i = tid; i < n4; i += blockDim.x) {
        float4 v4 = x4[i];
        if (v4.x > max_val) { max_val = v4.x; max_idx = (i << 2);     }
        if (v4.y > max_val) { max_val = v4.y; max_idx = (i << 2) + 1; }
        if (v4.z > max_val) { max_val = v4.z; max_idx = (i << 2) + 2; }
        if (v4.w > max_val) { max_val = v4.w; max_idx = (i << 2) + 3; }
    }
    for (int i = (n4 << 2) + tid; i < n; i += blockDim.x) {
        float v = row_x[i];
        if (v > max_val) { max_val = v; max_idx = i; }
    }

    unsigned mask = 0xFFFFFFFFu;
    #pragma unroll
    for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {
        float other_val = __shfl_down_sync(mask, max_val, offset);
        int   other_idx = __shfl_down_sync(mask, max_idx, offset);
        if (other_val > max_val) { max_val = other_val; max_idx = other_idx; }
    }

    __shared__ float s_vals[32];
    __shared__ int   s_idxs[32];
    int lane = tid & 31;
    int warp_id = tid >> 5;
    if (lane == 0) {
        s_vals[warp_id] = max_val;
        s_idxs[warp_id] = max_idx;
    }
    __syncthreads();

    if (tid < 32) {
        float v = (tid < ((blockDim.x + 31) >> 5)) ? s_vals[tid] : -FLT_MAX;
        int   idx = (tid < ((blockDim.x + 31) >> 5)) ? s_idxs[tid] : -1;
        #pragma unroll
        for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {
            float other_val = __shfl_down_sync(mask, v, offset);
            int   other_idx = __shfl_down_sync(mask, idx, offset);
            if (other_val > v) { v = other_val; idx = other_idx; }
        }
        if (tid == 0) {
            out_vals[row] = v;
            out_idxs[row] = idx;
        }
    }
}

template <int K>
__global__ void topk_pass1(const float* __restrict__ x,
                           float* __restrict__ tmp_vals,
                           int* __restrict__ tmp_idxs,
                           int n, int num_blocks_per_row, int M_PAD) {
    int row = blockIdx.x / num_blocks_per_row;
    int block_in_row = blockIdx.x % num_blocks_per_row;

    int block_start = block_in_row * n / num_blocks_per_row;
    int block_end   = (block_in_row + 1) * n / num_blocks_per_row;
    int block_n     = block_end - block_start;

    const float* row_x = x + row * n + block_start;

    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    int lane = tid & 31;
    int warp_id = tid >> 5;
    int num_warps = blockDim.x >> 5;
    int EPT = (block_n + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x;

    extern __shared__ char smem[];
    float* s_warp_vals = reinterpret_cast<float*>(smem);
    int*   s_warp_idxs = reinterpret_cast<int*>(smem + num_warps * M_PAD * sizeof(float));
    float* s_block_vals = reinterpret_cast<float*>(
        smem + num_warps * M_PAD * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int)));
    int* s_block_idxs = reinterpret_cast<int*>(s_block_vals + num_warps * K);

    for (int i = tid; i < num_warps * M_PAD; i += blockDim.x) {
        s_warp_vals[i] = -FLT_MAX;
    }
    __syncthreads();

    int warp_chunk = (block_n + num_warps - 1) / num_warps;
    int warp_start = warp_id * warp_chunk;
    int warp_end   = min(warp_start + warp_chunk, block_n);
    int warp_off   = warp_id * M_PAD;

    for (int j = 0; j < EPT; ++j) {
        int idx = warp_start + j * 32 + lane;
        float val = -FLT_MAX;
        int   idx_out = -1;
        if (idx < warp_end) {
            val = row_x[idx];
            idx_out = block_start + idx;
        }
        int smem_pos = j * 32 + lane;
        if (smem_pos < M_PAD) {
            s_warp_vals[warp_off + smem_pos] = val;
            s_warp_idxs[warp_off + smem_pos] = idx_out;
        }
    }
    __syncwarp();

    float* my_warp_vals = s_warp_vals + warp_off;
    int*   my_warp_idxs = s_warp_idxs + warp_off;
    for (int kk = 2; kk <= M_PAD; kk <<= 1) {
        for (int j = kk >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) {
            for (int i = lane; i < M_PAD; i += 32) {
                int ixj = i ^ j;
                if (ixj > i) {
                    if ((i & kk) == 0) {
                        if (my_warp_vals[i] < my_warp_vals[ixj]) {
                            float tv = my_warp_vals[i]; my_warp_vals[i] = my_warp_vals[ixj]; my_warp_vals[ixj] = tv;
                            int ti = my_warp_idxs[i]; my_warp_idxs[i] = my_warp_idxs[ixj]; my_warp_idxs[ixj] = ti;
                        }
                    } else {
                        if (my_warp_vals[i] > my_warp_vals[ixj]) {
                            float tv = my_warp_vals[i]; my_warp_vals[i] = my_warp_vals[ixj]; my_warp_vals[ixj] = tv;
                            int ti = my_warp_idxs[i]; my_warp_idxs[i] = my_warp_idxs[ixj]; my_warp_idxs[ixj] = ti;
                        }
                    }
                }
            }
            __syncwarp();
        }
    }

    if (lane < K) {
        s_block_vals[warp_id * K + lane] = my_warp_vals[lane];
        s_block_idxs[warp_id * K + lane] = my_warp_idxs[lane];
    }
    __syncthreads();

    int block_cand = num_warps * K;
    for (int kk = 2; kk <= block_cand; kk <<= 1) {
        for (int j = kk >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) {
            for (int i = tid; i < block_cand; i += blockDim.x) {
                int ixj = i ^ j;
                if (ixj > i) {
                    if ((i & kk) == 0) {
                        if (s_block_vals[i] < s_block_vals[ixj]) {
                            float tv = s_block_vals[i]; s_block_vals[i] = s_block_vals[ixj]; s_block_vals[ixj] = tv;
                            int ti = s_block_idxs[i]; s_block_idxs[i] = s_block_idxs[ixj]; s_block_idxs[ixj] = ti;
                        }
                    } else {
                        if (s_block_vals[i] > s_block_vals[ixj]) {
                            float tv = s_block_vals[i]; s_block_vals[i] = s_block_vals[ixj]; s_block_vals[ixj] = tv;
                            int ti = s_block_idxs[i]; s_block_idxs[i] = s_block_idxs[ixj]; s_block_idxs[ixj] = ti;
                        }
                    }
                }
            }
            __syncthreads();
        }
    }

    if (tid < K) {
        int out_pos = (row * num_blocks_per_row + block_in_row) * K + tid;
        tmp_vals[out_pos] = s_block_vals[tid];
        tmp_idxs[out_pos] = s_block_idxs[tid];
    }
}

template <int K, int CHUNK>
__global__ void topk_reduce(const float* __restrict__ in_vals,
                            const int* __restrict__ in_idxs,
                            float* __restrict__ out_vals,
                            int* __restrict__ out_idxs,
                            int num_candidates) {
    int row = blockIdx.y;
    int block_in_row = blockIdx.x;

    int chunk_start = block_in_row * CHUNK;
    int chunk_n = min(CHUNK, num_candidates - chunk_start);

    const float* row_vals = in_vals + row * num_candidates + chunk_start;
    const int*   row_idxs = in_idxs + row * num_candidates + chunk_start;

    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    int lane = tid & 31;
    int warp_id = tid >> 5;
    int num_warps = blockDim.x >> 5;
    int warp_chunk = CHUNK / num_warps;

    extern __shared__ char smem[];
    float* s_vals = reinterpret_cast<float*>(smem);
    int*   s_idxs = reinterpret_cast<int*>(smem + CHUNK * sizeof(float));
    float* s_out_vals = reinterpret_cast<float*>(smem + CHUNK * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int)));
    int*   s_out_idxs = reinterpret_cast<int*>(s_out_vals + num_warps * K);

    for (int i = tid; i < CHUNK; i += blockDim.x) {
        if (i < chunk_n) {
            s_vals[i] = row_vals[i];
            s_idxs[i] = row_idxs[i];
        } else {
            s_vals[i] = -FLT_MAX;
            s_idxs[i] = -1;
        }
    }
    __syncthreads();

    float* my_vals = s_vals + warp_id * warp_chunk;
    int*   my_idxs = s_idxs + warp_id * warp_chunk;

    for (int iter = 0; iter < K; ++iter) {
        float local_max = -FLT_MAX;
        int   local_pos = -1;
        for (int i = lane; i < warp_chunk; i += 32) {
            if (my_vals[i] > local_max) {
                local_max = my_vals[i];
                local_pos = i;
            }
        }
        #pragma unroll
        for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {
            float other_max = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFFu, local_max, offset);
            int   other_pos = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFFu, local_pos, offset);
            if (other_max > local_max) {
                local_max = other_max;
                local_pos = other_pos;
            }
        }
        int winner_pos = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFFu, local_pos, 0);
        float winner_val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFFu, local_max, 0);
        int  winner_idx  = my_idxs[winner_pos];

        if (lane == 0) {
            s_out_vals[warp_id * K + iter] = winner_val;
            s_out_idxs[warp_id * K + iter] = winner_idx;
        }
        if (lane == (winner_pos & 31)) {
            my_vals[winner_pos] = -FLT_MAX;
        }
        __syncwarp();
    }
    __syncthreads();

    int block_cand = num_warps * K;
    for (int kk = 2; kk <= block_cand; kk <<= 1) {
        for (int j = kk >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) {
            for (int i = tid; i < block_cand; i += blockDim.x) {
                int ixj = i ^ j;
                if (ixj > i) {
                    if ((i & kk) == 0) {
                        if (s_out_vals[i] < s_out_vals[ixj]) {
                            float tv = s_out_vals[i]; s_out_vals[i] = s_out_vals[ixj]; s_out_vals[ixj] = tv;
                            int ti = s_out_idxs[i]; s_out_idxs[i] = s_out_idxs[ixj]; s_out_idxs[ixj] = ti;
                        }
                    } else {
                        if (s_out_vals[i] > s_out_vals[ixj]) {
                            float tv = s_out_vals[i]; s_out_vals[i] = s_out_vals[ixj]; s_out_vals[ixj] = tv;
                            int ti = s_out_idxs[i]; s_out_idxs[i] = s_out_idxs[ixj]; s_out_idxs[ixj] = ti;
                        }
                    }
                }
            }
            __syncthreads();
        }
    }

    if (tid < K) {
        int out_pos = (row * gridDim.x + block_in_row) * K + tid;
        out_vals[out_pos] = s_out_vals[tid];
        out_idxs[out_pos] = s_out_idxs[tid];
    }
}

__global__ void copy_output(const float* __restrict__ in_vals,
                            const int* __restrict__ in_idxs,
                            float* __restrict__ out_vals,
                            int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs,
                            int n) {
    int i = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;
    if (i < n) {
        out_vals[i] = in_vals[i];
        out_idxs[i] = static_cast<int64_t>(in_idxs[i]);
    }
}

std::vector<torch::Tensor> topk_forward_cuda(torch::Tensor x, int k,
    int num_blocks_per_row,
    torch::Tensor tmp_a_vals, torch::Tensor tmp_a_idxs,
    torch::Tensor tmp_b_vals, torch::Tensor tmp_b_idxs) {

    int batch = x.size(0);
    int n     = x.size(1);

    auto out_vals = torch::empty({batch, k}, x.options());
    auto out_idxs = torch::empty({batch, k}, torch::dtype(torch::kInt64).device(x.device()));

    const float* x_ptr = x.data_ptr<float>();
    float*       v_ptr = out_vals.data_ptr<float>();
    int64_t*     i_ptr = out_idxs.data_ptr<int64_t>();

    if (k == 1) {
        topk1_kernel<<<batch, 256>>>(x_ptr, v_ptr, i_ptr, n);
        return {out_vals, out_idxs};
    }

    int total_blocks = batch * num_blocks_per_row;

    int block_n = (n + num_blocks_per_row - 1) / num_blocks_per_row;
    int EPT = (block_n + 127) / 128;
    int M = 32 * EPT;
    int M_PAD = 1;
    while (M_PAD < M) M_PAD <<= 1;

    // ----- Pass 1 -----
    int threads1 = 128;
    int num_warps1 = threads1 >> 5;
    size_t smem1 = num_warps1 * M_PAD * sizeof(float)
                 + num_warps1 * M_PAD * sizeof(int)
                 + num_warps1 * k * sizeof(float)
                 + num_warps1 * k * sizeof(int);

    float* a_v = tmp_a_vals.data_ptr<float>();
    int*   a_i = tmp_a_idxs.data_ptr<int>();
    float* b_v = tmp_b_vals.data_ptr<float>();
    int*   b_i = tmp_b_idxs.data_ptr<int>();

    switch (k) {
        case 8:  topk_pass1<8> <<<total_blocks, threads1, smem1>>>(x_ptr, a_v, a_i, n, num_blocks_per_row, M_PAD); break;
        case 16: topk_pass1<16><<<total_blocks, threads1, smem1>>>(x_ptr, a_v, a_i, n, num_blocks_per_row, M_PAD); break;
        case 32: topk_pass1<32><<<total_blocks, threads1, smem1>>>(x_ptr, a_v, a_i, n, num_blocks_per_row, M_PAD); break;
        case 64: topk_pass1<64><<<total_blocks, threads1, smem1>>>(x_ptr, a_v, a_i, n, num_blocks_per_row, M_PAD); break;
    }

    // ----- Pass 2 : iterative reduction -----
    const int CHUNK = 1024;
    int threads2 = 256;
    int num_warps2 = threads2 >> 5;
    size_t smem2 = CHUNK * sizeof(float)
                 + CHUNK * sizeof(int)
                 + num_warps2 * k * sizeof(float)
                 + num_warps2 * k * sizeof(int);

    float* in_v = a_v;
    int*   in_i = a_i;
    float* out_v = b_v;
    int*   out_i = b_i;

    int num_candidates = num_blocks_per_row * k;
    while (num_candidates > k) {
        int blocks_per_row = (num_candidates + CHUNK - 1) / CHUNK;
        dim3 grid(blocks_per_row, batch);

        switch (k) {
            case 8:  topk_reduce<8,  CHUNK><<<grid, threads2, smem2>>>(in_v, in_i, out_v, out_i, num_candidates); break;
            case 16: topk_reduce<16, CHUNK><<<grid, threads2, smem2>>>(in_v, in_i, out_v, out_i, num_candidates); break;
            case 32: topk_reduce<32, CHUNK><<<grid, threads2, smem2>>>(in_v, in_i, out_v, out_i, num_candidates); break;
            case 64: topk_reduce<64, CHUNK><<<grid, threads2, smem2>>>(in_v, in_i, out_v, out_i, num_candidates); break;
        }

        num_candidates = blocks_per_row * k;
        std::swap(in_v, out_v);
        std::swap(in_i, out_i);
    }

    // copy final int indices to int64 output
    int total_out = batch * k;
    int copy_threads = 256;
    int copy_blocks = (total_out + copy_threads - 1) / copy_threads;
    copy_output<<<copy_blocks, copy_threads>>>(in_v, in_i, v_ptr, i_ptr, total_out);

    return {out_vals, out_idxs};
}
"""

_topk_cuda = load_inline(
    name="topk_cuda_v9",
    cpp_sources=CPP_SOURCE,
    cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE,
    functions=["topk_forward_cuda"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math", "-gencode=arch=compute_120,code=sm_120"],
    with_cuda=True,
    verbose=False,
)


class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, batch: int, n: int, k: int):
        super().__init__()
        self.batch, self.n, self.k = batch, n, k
        self.num_blocks_per_row = max(1, 256 // batch)
        self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1))

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor):
        if self.k == 1:
            return _topk_cuda.topk_forward_cuda(x, self.k, 0, self._dummy, self._dummy, self._dummy, self._dummy)

        # Lazily allocate temp buffers on the correct device
        if not hasattr(self, "_tmp_a_vals") or self._tmp_a_vals.device != x.device:
            max_candidates = self.num_blocks_per_row * self.k
            self._tmp_a_vals = torch.empty(self.batch, max_candidates, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device)
            self._tmp_a_idxs = torch.empty(self.batch, max_candidates, dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device)
            self._tmp_b_vals = torch.empty(self.batch, max_candidates, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device)
            self._tmp_b_idxs = torch.empty(self.batch, max_candidates, dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device)

        return _topk_cuda.topk_forward_cuda(
            x, self.k, self.num_blocks_per_row,
            self._tmp_a_vals, self._tmp_a_idxs,
            self._tmp_b_vals, self._tmp_b_idxs,
        )

Let me design the kernel more carefully. **First Pass Kernel:** ```cuda template<int K> __global__ void local_topk(const float* x, float* out_vals, int64_t* out_idxs, int n, int k) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int nthreads = blockDim.x; const float* row_x = x + row * n; // Each thread maintains top-K elements float vals[K]; int64_t idxs[K]; // Initialize with -inf #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { vals[i] = -INFINITY; idxs[i] = -1; } // Strided loop over elements for (int i = tid; i < n; i += nthreads) { float v = row_x[i]; // Insert into sorted array if v > vals[K-1] if (v > vals[K-1]) { // Shift and insert int pos = K - 1; while (pos > 0 && v > vals[pos-1]) { vals[pos] = vals[pos-1]; idxs[pos] = idxs[pos-1]; pos--; } vals[pos] = v; idxs[pos] = i; } } // Write to global memory float* row_out_vals = out_vals + row * nthreads * k; int64_t* row_out_idxs = out_idxs + row * nthreads * k; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { row_out_vals[tid * k + i] = vals[i]; row_out_idxs[tid * k + i] = idxs[i]; } } ``` **Second Pass Kernel:** ```cuda template<int K> __global__ void merge_topk(const float* in_vals, const int64_t* in_idxs, float* out_vals, int64_t* out_idxs, int num_candidates, int k) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Load all candidates into shared memory extern __shared__ char smem[]; float* s_vals = (float*)smem; int64_t* s_idxs = (int64_t*)(smem + num_candidates * sizeof(float)); const float* row_in_vals = in_vals + row * num_candidates; const int64_t* row_in_idxs = in_idxs + row * num_candidates; for (int i = tid; i < num_candidates; i += blockDim.x) { s_vals[i] = row_in_vals[i]; s_idxs[i] = row_in_idxs[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Bitonic sort in shared memory - only need top K // ... sort s_vals/s_idxs descending, then write top K } ``` The bitonic sort for the second pass needs to handle arbitrary `num_candidates`. The performance difference is minimal for shared memory sorts. Loop-based bitonic sort: ```cuda for (int size = 2; size <= N; size *= 2) { for (int stride = size / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { // Compare-and-swap elements for (int i = tid; i < N/2; i += blockDim.x) { // calculate indices to compare int idx = i * 2 + ((i / stride) % 2) * stride; // Actually, standard bitonic sort indexing... } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, standard bitonic sort indexing for descending order: For each pair of elements to compare: ```cuda int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { if ((i & k2) == 0) { // ascending in this phase... actually for descending: if (s_vals[i] < s_vals[ixj]) swap; } else { if (s_vals[i] > s_vals[ixj]) swap; } } ``` Let me write a cleaner bitonic sort. The standard algorithm for descending: ```cuda // N must be power of 2 for (int k = 2; k <= N; k *= 2) { for (int j = k / 2; j > 0; j /= 2) { for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { bool ascending = (i & k) == 0; // For descending overall, we want: // In first half of k-block: larger values go to lower indices // In second half: larger values go to higher indices... wait } } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Actually, let me be careful. For a descending sort (largest first), the standard bitonic sort works as follows: In the outer loop over k = 2, 4, 8, ..., N: - For each k, we create bitonic sequences of length k - The first k/2 elements should be in ascending order, the second k/2 in descending (or vice versa, depending on position) For a fully descending sort: - At each stage k, elements are divided into blocks of size k - Within each block, we want the first half sorted ascending and second half descending (forming a bitonic sequence) - Then we merge Actually, let me just use the well-known indexing. For descending order: ```cuda for (int k = 2; k <= N; k = k * 2) { for (int j = k / 2; j > 0; j = j / 2) { for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { if ((i & k) == 0) { if (s_vals[i] < s_vals[ixj]) { swap(s_vals[i], s_vals[ixj]); swap(s_idxs[i], s_idxs[ixj]); } } else { if (s_vals[i] > s_vals[ixj]) { swap(s_vals[i], s_vals[ixj]); swap(s_idxs[i], s_idxs[ixj]); } } } } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, I need to double-check this. For k=2, j=1: - i=0: ixj=1, (0 & 2)==0, so if s[0] < s[1], swap -> s[0] > s[1] (descending pair) - i=1: ixj=0, but ixj > i is false, so skip - i=2: ixj=3, (2 & 2)!=0, so if s[2] > s[3], swap -> s[2] < s[3] (ascending pair) - i=3: ixj=2, skip After k=2: pairs are [desc, asc, desc, asc, ...] - this is correct for bitonic sequence building. For k=4, j=2: - i=0: ixj=2, (0&4)==0, if s[0] < s[2], swap -> larger to 0 - i=1: ixj=3, (1&4)==0, if s[1] < s[3], swap -> larger to 1 - i=2: ixj=0, skip - i=3: ixj=1, skip - i=4: ixj=6, (4&4)!=0, if s[4] > s[6], swap -> smaller to 4 - i=5: ixj=7, (5&4)!=0, if s[5] > s[7], swap -> smaller to 5 After j=2: within each 4-block, first half has larger elements from first&second quarters, second half has smaller. Then j=1: - i=0: ixj=1, (0&4)==0, if s[0] < s[1], swap - i=2: ixj=3, (2&4)==0, if s[2] < s[3], swap - i=4: ixj=5, (4&4)!=0, if s[4] > s[5], swap - i=6: ixj=7, (6&4)!=0, if s[6] > s[7], swap After k=4: each 4-block is fully descending. Great! So the code above produces a fully descending sort. Good. Now, each thread accesses elements i and ixj. When i and ixj are far apart, this can cause bank conflicts if they're in the same bank. But for float4 or float, adjacent threads access adjacent elements, so there might be some conflicts. Actually, with 32 banks and stride j, if j is a multiple of 32, all threads in a warp access the same bank -> major conflict. To avoid bank conflicts, we can use a different indexing scheme or use shared memory with padding. But for small sorts in shared memory, bank conflicts might not be terrible. Actually, for j >= 32, all threads in a warp read the same bank but different addresses. Wait, no - for j=32, thread i reads bank (i % 32) and bank ((i^32) % 32) = bank (i % 32). So yes, both reads are from the same bank! That's a 2-way conflict for all threads. One fix is to pad the shared memory array so that index i maps to bank (i * PADDING % 32). For example, with PADDING=33, adjacent elements are in different banks. But this makes indexing more complex. Another fix is to have each thread process two adjacent elements that are compared. But in bitonic sort, the comparison distance varies. For the sizes we're dealing with (up to 16384), the number of stages with j >= 32 is small (log2(16384) - 5 = 9 stages). Bank conflicts in these stages might not be a huge issue. Alternatively, I can use a "shuffled" indexing to avoid conflicts. But let's keep it simple first and optimize if needed. Now let me think about the overall structure of solution.py. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # CUDA source code cuda_source = """ ... kernels ... """ # Compile module = load_inline( name="topk_cuda", cpp_sources="", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["topk_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], ) class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, batch, n, k): super().__init__() self.batch = batch self.n = n self.k = k self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1)) def forward(self, x): return module.topk_forward(x, self.k) ``` Wait, I need to handle the case where k varies. But for a given model instance, k is fixed. For the kernel dispatch, I'll have: - A specialized path for k=1 (argmax) - A general two-pass path for k > 1 Actually, for k=1, the two-pass approach works too (candidates = 256, sort 256 elements to get top 1). But an argmax reduction is much faster. Let me implement both. For k=1: ```cuda __global__ void topk1_kernel(const float* x, float* out_vals, int64_t* out_idxs, int n) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % 32; int warp = tid / 32; const float* row_x = x + row * n; float max_val = -INFINITY; int max_idx = -1; for (int i = tid; i < n; i += blockDim.x) { float v = row_x[i]; if (v > max_val) { max_val = v; max_idx = i; } } // Warp reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { float other_val = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_val, offset); int other_idx = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_idx, offset); if (other_val > max_val) { max_val = other_val; max_idx = other_idx; } } // Write warp results to shared memory __shared__ float s_vals[32]; __shared__ int s_idxs[32]; if (lane == 0) { s_vals[warp] = max_val; s_idxs[warp] = max_idx; } __syncthreads(); // Final reduction by warp 0 if (tid < 32) { float v = (tid < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32) ? s_vals[tid] : -INFINITY; int idx = (tid < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32) ? s_idxs[tid] : -1; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { float other_val = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v, offset); int other_idx = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, idx, offset); if (other_val > v) { v = other_val; idx = other_idx; } } if (tid == 0) { out_vals[row] = v; out_idxs[row] = idx; } } } ``` For k > 1, the two-pass approach. Let me write a templated version for the first pass. Actually, since k can be 8, 16, 32, 64, I can template on k for better performance. But CUDA kernels with template parameters need to be instantiated. I can use `switch(k)` to dispatch to template instantiations. Let me draft the full CUDA code. For the first pass template: ```cuda template <int K> __global__ void local_topk_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ tmp_vals, int* __restrict__ tmp_idxs, int n, int k) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int nthreads = blockDim.x; const float* row_x = x + row * n; float vals[K]; int idxs[K]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { vals[i] = -INFINITY; idxs[i] = -1; } for (int i = tid; i < n; i += nthreads) { float v = row_x[i]; if (v > vals[K-1]) { int pos = K - 1; while (pos > 0 && v > vals[pos-1]) { vals[pos] = vals[pos-1]; idxs[pos] = idxs[pos-1]; pos--; } vals[pos] = v; idxs[pos] = i; } } float* row_tmp_vals = tmp_vals + row * nthreads * k; int* row_tmp_idxs = tmp_idxs + row * nthreads * k; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { row_tmp_vals[tid * k + i] = vals[i]; row_tmp_idxs[tid * k + i] = idxs[i]; } } ``` For the second pass, bitonic sort: ```cuda template <int K> __global__ void merge_topk_kernel(const float* __restrict__ tmp_vals, const int* __restrict__ tmp_idxs, float* __restrict__ out_vals, int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs, int n_candidates, int k) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; extern __shared__ char smem[]; float* s_vals = (float*)smem; int* s_idxs = (int*)(smem + n_candidates * sizeof(float)); const float* row_tmp_vals = tmp_vals + row * n_candidates; const int* row_tmp_idxs = tmp_idxs + row * n_candidates; for (int i = tid; i < n_candidates; i += blockDim.x) { s_vals[i] = row_tmp_vals[i]; s_idxs[i] = row_tmp_idxs[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Bitonic sort descending for (int kk = 2; kk <= n_candidates; kk *= 2) { for (int j = kk / 2; j > 0; j /= 2) { for (int i = tid; i < n_candidates; i += blockDim.x) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { if ((i & kk) == 0) { if (s_vals[i] < s_vals[ixj]) { float tmp_v = s_vals[i]; s_vals[i] = s_vals[ixj]; s_vals[ixj] = tmp_v; int tmp_i = s_idxs[i]; s_idxs[i] = s_idxs[ixj]; s_idxs[ixj] = tmp_i; } } else { if (s_vals[i] > s_vals[ixj]) { float tmp_v = s_vals[i]; s_vals[i] = s_vals[ixj]; s_vals[ixj] = tmp_v; int tmp_i = s_idxs[i]; s_idxs[i] = s_idxs[ixj]; s_idxs[ixj] = tmp_i; } } } } __syncthreads(); } } // Write top K if (tid < k) { out_vals[row * k + tid] = s_vals[tid]; out_idxs[row * k + tid] = (int64_t)s_idxs[tid]; } } ``` Wait, there's an issue with shared memory size. The extern shared memory is allocated per block, and I need to tell the kernel how much to allocate. Let me write the C++ wrapper: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> // ... kernel declarations ... torch::Tensor topk_forward(torch::Tensor x, int k) { int batch = x.size(0); } } return {vals, idxs}; } ``` Wait, actually I should be returning a tuple from the C++ function. In PyTorch C++ extensions: ```cpp std::vector<torch::Tensor> topk_forward(torch::Tensor x, int k) { // ... return {vals, idxs}; } ``` And in Python: ```python module = load_inline( name="topk_cuda", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["topk_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], with_cuda=True, ) ``` Now let me think about optimizing the first pass. The insertion sort in registers might not be optimal. For small K, unrolling the insertion is important. For K=8, each insertion requires up to 8 comparisons and shifts. For 512 elements per thread (n=131072, threads=256), that's 512 * 8 = 4096 operations per thread. At 1.5GHz, that's ~2.7 microseconds. With 256 threads, the block takes ~2.7 microseconds (they run in parallel). Total for batch=1: ~2.7 microseconds + memory reads. Actually, the memory read of 512KB at 1.8 TB/s is ~0.28 microseconds. But in practice, with latency and coalescing, it's probably 1-2 microseconds. So the compute might be comparable. Wait, 256 threads can saturate memory bandwidth if they're all reading. For 512KB, with perfect coalescing, each warp reads 128 bytes per instruction. That's 512KB / 128B = 4096 warp-instructions. At 1 warp/SM/clock (roughly), with 128 warps total (4 blocks of 32 warps on a large GPU), this might take a few microseconds. Hmm, let me think about whether I can optimize further. Actually, for the first pass, instead of maintaining a sorted array, I could use a different approach: 1. Process elements in groups of K 2. Sort each group of K using a sorting network 3. Merge with the current top-K using a merge network But for K=64, this might be more complex than simple insertion. Another idea: Use vectorized loads. Each thread loads 4 floats at a time (float4). It's a common optimization: ```cuda float4* x4 = (float4*)row_x; int n4 = n / 4; for (int i = tid; i < n4; i += nthreads) { float4 v4 = x4[i]; // process v4.x, v4.y, v4.z, v4.w } // Handle remaining elements for (int i = tid + n4 * 4; i < n; i += nthreads) { float v = row_x[i]; // process v } ``` This is good! Let me incorporate this. Now, for the second pass, the bitonic sort in shared memory. The issue is that with many threads accessing shared memory with varying strides, bank conflicts can occur. For j = 1: threads access i and i^1, which are adjacent -> no bank conflict (different banks) For j = 2: access i and i^2, stride 2 -> might have some conflicts depending on bank size Actually, for 32-byte banks (as in modern GPUs), float is 4 bytes, so 8 floats per bank. Stride j=1 means accessing elements 1 apart, which is 4 bytes apart. With 32 threads in a warp, thread i accesses banks (i % 8) and ((i^1) % 8). For even i, (i^1) = i+1, so banks are i%8 and (i+1)%8 -> different banks. For odd i, same but reversed. No conflicts. For j=2: i and i^2. For i=0: 0 and 2, banks 0 and 2. i=1: 1 and 3, banks 1 and 3. i=2: 2 and 0, same as i=0. Wait, but threads 0 and 2 both access banks 0 and 2. Are there conflicts? Thread 0 accesses shared[0] and shared[2]. Thread 1 accesses shared[1] and shared[3]. Thread 2 accesses shared[2] and shared[0]. Thread 3 accesses shared[3] and shared[1]. In a warp, threads 0,1,2,3 are active. Bank 0 is accessed by thread 0 (shared[0]) and thread 2 (shared[0] via shared[2]'s partner). So yes, bank conflict! For j=4: even worse. Many threads access the same banks. This is a well-known issue with bitonic sort. One solution is to pad the shared memory array so that index i maps to a different bank than index i^j for all j. A common padding is: ```cuda // Instead of s[i], use s[i + (i >> 5)] // Or use a struct with padding ``` Actually, a simpler fix for small N (like up to 16384) is to not worry too much about it. The number of stages with significant conflicts is small, and shared memory is fast anyway. But we can optimize: store values and indices in a struct and add padding: ```cuda struct Item { float val; int idx; }; __shared__ Item s_items[N + N/32]; // pad every 32 elements ``` With padding, index i maps to `i + (i >> 5)` or similar. Let me use `i + (i / 32)` as the padded index. Actually, a cleaner way: `s[i + (i >> 5)]` gives one extra element every 32 elements, shifting elements 32+ apart by 1, which changes the bank. Hmm, this is getting complicated. Let me just use the simple version first and optimize if profiling shows it's a bottleneck. Actually, wait. Let me check. Actually, for safety, I can use a two-level approach within one kernel: 1. Each warp (32 threads) processes its portion and keeps top-k 2. Warp-level shuffle to find top-k per warp 3. Then block-level merge of warp results For 256 threads = 8 warps. Each warp keeps top-k. Then we merge 8 top-k lists. For k=64, merging 8 lists of 64 into top-64: - Load all 512 candidates into shared memory - Bitonic sort 512 elements (padded to 512) -> 512 * 9 * 9 / 2 = ~20K comparisons - Take top-64 Shared memory: 512 * 4 + 512 * 4 = 4KB (with int32 indices). Very small! Wait, that's brilliant! Here's the single-kernel approach: **Warp-local top-k + block-level merge** 1. Divide block into warps (8 warps of 32 threads for 256 threads) 2. Each warp processes its strided elements: - Warp w processes elements starting at w*32 + lane, then +256, +512, etc. - Each thread in warp keeps a local top-k in registers - Within warp, merge all 32 top-k lists into one warp top-k list using warp shuffles 3. After all warps have their top-k, write all warp top-k lists to shared memory 4. One warp (or all threads) bitonic sorts the candidates and extracts top-k Let me detail step 2 - warp-level merge: Each thread in the warp has top-k in registers. To merge 32 top-k lists into one: - Write all 32*k candidates to shared memory (within warp) - Bitonic sort 32*k elements (padded to next power of 2) - Each thread reads back top-k For k=64, 32*k = 2048. Shared memory per warp: 2048 * 4 + 2048 * 4 = 16KB. For 8 warps, if they use shared memory sequentially (not simultaneously), total is still 16KB. Or we can partition shared memory into 8 regions of 16KB each = 128KB total. Actually, warps in a block can share shared memory. We can have 8 * 2048 = 16384 elements total = 128KB. That's the same as before. But the key insight: instead of having each thread keep top-k and then sorting 16384 elements at the block level, we first merge within warps to reduce 256 top-k lists to 8 top-k lists. Then we sort 512 elements at the block level instead of 16384. That's 32x fewer elements to sort at the block level! Let me do this. **Detailed warp-level merge:** Within a warp, each of 32 threads has a sorted top-k array `vals[K]`, `idxs[K]`. To merge these into a single top-k array: 1. Write all 32*K candidates to shared memory (warp-private region) 2. Bitonic sort the 32*K candidates 3. Thread 0 reads top-k and broadcasts to all threads in warp... or each thread reads its portion of top-k Actually, after sorting, we only need the top-k. Or we can have thread 0 keep the full top-k and the rest just continue processing. Wait, in this design, the warp merge happens after all elements are processed. Each warp first processes all its assigned elements (strided by blockDim.x), building thread-local top-k. Then merges within the warp. Then writes warp top-k to block shared memory. Then block-level bitonic sort on warp results. For step 2 (warp merge), I can do it like this: ```cuda // Within warp, merge 32 top-k lists __shared__ float s_warp_vals[32][128]; // 8 warps * 2048 max candidates, but let's use dynamic __shared__ int s_warp_idxs[32][128]; ``` Actually, let's use a simpler approach. I'll allocate shared memory for all warps: ```cuda // Max candidates per warp = 32 * 64 = 2048 // 8 warps -> 16384 total elements // But we process warp by warp __shared__ float s_vals[WARP_MAX_CANDIDATES]; __shared__ int s_idxs[WARP_MAX_CANDIDATES]; ``` For merging within warp: ```cuda // Each thread writes its top-k for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { s_vals[lane * k + i] = vals[i]; s_idxs[lane * k + i] = idxs[i]; } __syncwarp(); // Bitonic sort 32*k candidates int warp_candidates = 32 * k; for (int kk = 2; kk <= warp_candidates; kk *= 2) { for (int j = kk / 2; j > 0; j /= 2) { for (int i = lane; i < warp_candidates; i += 32) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { if ((i & kk) == 0) { if (s_vals[i] < s_vals[ixj]) swap; } else { if (s_vals[i] > s_vals[ixj]) swap; } } } __syncwarp(); } } // Each thread reads back top-k portion for (int i = 0; i < k; i++) { vals[i] = s_vals[lane * k + i]; // Wait, top-k are at indices 0..k-1 idxs[i] = s_idxs[lane * k + i]; } ``` Wait, after sorting, the top-k are at indices 0 to k-1. I want all threads in the warp to have the top-k. But with 32 threads and k=64, each thread can hold 2 elements. Or I can have thread 0 hold all 64 in its registers and then... that's too many. Actually, I don't need each thread to hold all top-k after the warp merge. I just need the warp top-k to be written to shared memory, and then the block-level sort will pick it up. Here's a revised plan: 1. Each thread processes its chunk, keeps top-k in registers 2. Warp merge: all threads write to warp shared memory, sort, result is top-k in shared memory indices 0..k-1 3. Warp 0 (or a designated thread) copies warp top-k from shared memory to block shared memory at warp_offset 4. After all warps done, block-level bitonic sort on `num_warps * k` candidates 5. Write top-k to output For step 3, I need to coordinate. Let me think... Actually, a cleaner design: - After warp-level sort, the top-k are at s_vals[0..k-1] in the warp's shared memory region - Thread 0 of each warp copies these k elements to the block-level shared memory - All warps do this simultaneously (each to its own region) - Then __syncthreads() - Block-level bitonic sort on block_candidates = num_warps * k Shared memory layout: - Partition 1: warp working area (max 2048 floats + 2048 ints) = 16KB - Partition 2: block candidate area (8 * 64 = 512 floats + 512 ints) = 4KB Total: 20KB. Very manageable! Actually, we can reuse partition 1 as the block working area for the final sort. The block candidates (512 elements) can be stored in a separate small array. Or we can sort in place. Let me think about the control flow more carefully. ```cuda template <int K> __global__ void topk_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out_vals, int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs, int n, int k) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; int num_warps = blockDim.x >> 5; const float* row_x = x + row * n; // Thread-local top-K float vals[K]; int idxs[K]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { vals[i] = -INFINITY; idxs[i] = -1; } // Process elements for (int i = tid; i < n; i += blockDim.x) { float v = row_x[i]; if (v > vals[K-1]) { int pos = K - 1; while (pos > 0 && v > vals[pos-1]) { vals[pos] = vals[pos-1]; idxs[pos] = idxs[pos-1]; pos--; } vals[pos] = v; idxs[pos] = i; } } // Warp-level shared memory // Layout: [warp0_area][warp1_area]... but actually each warp uses it sequentially // Better: all warps use the same area but at different times? No, they run concurrently. // So partition by warp_id. extern __shared__ char smem[]; // Warp work area: each warp gets 32*K elements float* s_warp_vals = (float*)smem; int* s_warp_idxs = (int*)(smem + num_warps * 32 * K * sizeof(float)); float* my_warp_vals = s_warp_vals + warp_id * 32 * K; int* my_warp_idxs = s_warp_idxs + warp_id * 32 * K; // Write thread top-k to warp shared memory #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { my_warp_vals[lane * K + i] = vals[i]; my_warp_idxs[lane * K + i] = idxs[i]; } __syncwarp(); // Bitonic sort within warp int warp_cand = 32 * K; for (int kk = 2; kk <= warp_cand; kk *= 2) { for (int j = kk / 2; j > 0; j /= 2) { for (int i = lane; i < warp_cand; i += 32) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { bool dir = (i & kk) == 0; // true for ascending in bitonic, but we want... // For descending sort: // In first half of k-block: larger at lower index // In second half: larger at higher index if (dir) { // i in first half if (my_warp_vals[i] < my_warp_vals[ixj]) { swap(my_warp_vals[i], my_warp_vals[ixj]); swap(my_warp_idxs[i], my_warp_idxs[ixj]); } } else { if (my_warp_vals[i] > my_warp_vals[ixj]) { swap(my_warp_vals[i], my_warp_vals[ixj]); swap(my_warp_idxs[i], my_warp_idxs[ixj]); } } } } __syncwarp(); } } // Now top-K of warp are at my_warp_vals[0..K-1] // Copy warp top-K to block candidate area // Block candidate area is after warp work areas float* s_block_vals = (float*)(smem + num_warps * 32 * K * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int))); int* s_block_idxs = (int*)(s_block_vals + num_warps * K); if (lane < K) { s_block_vals[warp_id * K + lane] = my_warp_vals[lane]; s_block_idxs[warp_id * K + lane] = my_warp_idxs[lane]; } __syncthreads(); // Block-level bitonic sort on num_warps * K candidates int block_cand = num_warps * K; if (tid < block_cand) { // Only need block_cand threads, but all can participate for (int kk = 2; kk <= block_cand; kk *= 2) { for (int j = kk / 2; j > 0; j /= 2) { for (int i = tid; i < block_cand; i += blockDim.x) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { bool dir = (i & kk) == 0; if (dir) { if (s_block_vals[i] < s_block_vals[ixj]) { swap(s_block_vals[i], s_block_vals[ixj]); swap(s_block_idxs[i], s_block_idxs[ixj]); } } else { if (s_block_vals[i] > s_block_vals[ixj]) { swap(s_block_vals[i], s_block_vals[ixj]); swap(s_block_idxs[i], s_block_idxs[ixj]); } } } } __syncthreads(); } } } // Write output if (tid < k) { out_vals[row * k + tid] = s_block_vals[tid]; out_idxs[row * k + tid] = (int64_t)s_block_idxs[tid]; } } ``` Wait, there's a subtle issue. In the block-level sort, I'm using `tid < block_cand` as a condition inside the loop, but the `__syncthreads()` requires all threads in the block to reach it. So I can't have an `if (tid < block_cand)` around the `__syncthreads()`. I need all threads to participate in the block-level sort. For small block_cand (like 64), some threads will have `i >= block_cand` and do nothing, but they'll still hit `__syncthreads()`. That's fine. Actually, let me restructure: ```cuda for (int kk = 2; kk <= block_cand; kk *= 2) { for (int j = kk / 2; j > 0; j /= 2) { for (int i = tid; i < block_cand; i += blockDim.x) { // compare and maybe swap } __syncthreads(); } } ``` This works because all threads participate in the loop (even if their `i` is out of bounds, the loop just doesn't execute for them), and all hit `__syncthreads()`. The bitonic sort of 64 elements in registers: ```cuda // 64-element bitonic sort (descending) // Using CUDA's ability to swap registers #define CMP_SWAP(i, j) \ if (vals[i] < vals[j]) { \ float tmp_v = vals[i]; vals[i] = vals[j]; vals[j] = tmp_v; \ int tmp_i = idxs[i]; idxs[i] = idxs[j]; idxs[j] = tmp_i; \ } // Stage 1: pairs CMP_SWAP(0, 1); CMP_SWAP(2, 3); ... CMP_SWAP(62, 63); // Stage 2: 4-element sequences CMP_SWAP(0, 2); CMP_SWAP(1, 3); ... // ... ``` This is a lot of code but can be fully unrolled. Actually, total comparators for bitonic sort of N is (N * log2(N) * (log2(N)+1)) / 4 = 64 * 6 * 7 / 4 = 672 comparators. Each comparator is a compare and possible swap. With registers, this is very fast. But writing all this out is tedious. We can use a loop-based approach too: ```cuda for (int k = 2; k <= 64; k *= 2) { for (int j = k / 2; j > 0; j /= 2) { for (int i = 0; i < 64; i++) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { bool dir = (i & k) == 0; if (dir) { if (vals[i] < vals[ixj]) swap; } else { if (vals[i] > vals[ixj]) swap; } } } } } ``` This is much cleaner and the compiler will unroll it since 64 is a compile-time constant (if K is a template parameter and we use a constexpr). Wait, K is a template parameter, but the loop bounds use `K` which is known at compile time. The compiler should unroll this nicely. For register operations, branches might not be terrible but they do cause some divergence within a thread (not within a warp, since all threads in the warp do the same thing). Wait, within a thread, the branch is deterministic based on `i` and `j`. The compiler might even optimize it away since `dir` is known at compile time for each unrolled iteration. Compare vals[2] > vals[3] - etc. The compiler can see all of this at compile time and generate straight-line code. Let me use the loop-based bitonic sort for register arrays. **Revised thread-level processing:** Instead of streaming insertion sort, I'll do: 1. Process elements in chunks of K 2. No. [descending, descending] is not bitonic. - But [descending, ascending_reversed] is bitonic. - If I reverse the new chunk, I get a bitonic sequence of size 2K. - Then I can bitonic merge it (log2(2K) stages) and take top K. Let me implement this. ```cuda template <int K> __device__ void bitonic_sort(float* vals, int* idxs, int n) { for (int k = 2; k <= n; k *= 2) { for (int j = k / 2; j > 0; j /= 2) { #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { bool dir = (i & k) == 0; if (dir) { if (vals[i] < vals[ixj]) { float tmp_v = vals[i]; vals[i] = vals[ixj]; vals[ixj] = tmp_v; int tmp_i = idxs[i]; idxs[i] = idxs[ixj]; idxs[j] = tmp_i; } } else { if (vals[i] > vals[ixj]) { float tmp_v = vals[i]; vals[i] = vals[ixj]; vals[ixj] = tmp_v; int tmp_i = idxs[i]; idxs[i] = idxs[ixj]; idxs[j] = tmp_i; } } } } } } } template <int K> __device__ void bitonic_merge_topk(float* topk_vals, int* topk_idxs, float* chunk_vals, int* chunk_idxs) { // chunk is already sorted descending // Reverse chunk to create bitonic sequence: [topk_desc, chunk_asc] float seq_vals[2*K]; int seq_idxs[2*K]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { seq_vals[i] = topk_vals[i]; seq_idxs[i] = topk_idxs[i]; seq_vals[K + i] = chunk_vals[K - 1 - i]; seq_idxs[K + i] = chunk_idxs[K - 1 - i]; } // Bitonic merge of size 2K for (int j = K; j > 0; j /= 2) { #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 2*K; i++) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { // For descending merge: first half of each 2j block keeps larger bool is_first_half = (i & (2*j)) == 0; if (is_first_half) { if (seq_vals[i] < seq_vals[ixj]) { swap } } else { if (seq_vals[i] > seq_vals[ixj]) { swap } } } } } // Copy top K back #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { topk_vals[i] = seq_vals[i]; topk_idxs[i] = seq_idxs[i]; } } ``` Wait, for the bitonic merge, I'm not sure about the direction logic. Let me think carefully. A bitonic sequence is one that first increases then decreases (or vice versa). [descending, ascending] is bitonic. The bitonic merge algorithm for a bitonic sequence of size N: 1. Compare elements i and i + N/2 for all i < N/2 2. If the sequence is [descending, ascending] and we want descending: - In the first half, keep the larger of each pair - In the second half, keep the smaller of each pair 3. Recursively merge each half Actually, the standard bitonic merge for descending: ```cuda for (int j = N/2; j > 0; j /= 2) { for (int i = 0; i < N; i++) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { if (i < (i | j)) { // or some condition // keep larger in lower half } } } } ``` Hmm, let me think about this more carefully. In a bitonic merge: - We compare i and i + j where j starts at N/2 and halves each iteration - For a descending sort, we want the larger elements in the first half after each comparison stage Actually, for the merge of a bitonic sequence into descending order: ```cuda for (int j = N/2; j > 0; j /= 2) { for (int i = 0; i < N; i++) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { // Determine direction based on position in the current sub-problem bool up = ((i / (2*j)) % 2) == 0; // first half of 2j block if (up) { if (vals[i] < vals[ixj]) swap; // larger goes to first half } else { if (vals[i] > vals[ixj]) swap; // smaller goes to second half } } } } ``` Wait, in the first iteration j = N/2. The 2j block is the whole N. So `up = ((i / N) % 2) == 0` is always true for i < N. So we always do `if (vals[i] < vals[ixj]) swap`. This means after the first stage, the larger of each pair (i, i+N/2) goes to position i. After this stage, we have two bitonic sequences of size N/2, each with larger elements in the first half. We recursively merge each half. For the second iteration j = N/4: - For i in [0, N/2): 2j block is [0, N/2) or [N/2, N). - `up = ((i / (N/2)) % 2) == 0`. For i in [0, N/2), this is true. For i in [N/2, N), this is false. - So for first half: keep larger. For second half: keep smaller. This is correct! So the merge code is: ```cuda for (int j = K; j > 0; j /= 2) { // K is N/2 where N=2K for (int i = 0; i < 2*K; i++) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { bool up = ((i / (2*j)) % 2) == 0; if (up) { if (seq_vals[i] < seq_vals[ixj]) swap; } else { if (seq_vals[i] > seq_vals[ixj]) swap; } } } } ``` Wait, for j=K, 2*j = 2*K = N. For all i in [0, N), i/N = 0, so up is always true. Good. For j=K/2, 2*j = K. For i in [0, K), i/K = 0, up=true. For i in [K, 2K), i/K = 1, up=false. Good. This looks correct! Now, for the chunk processing, I need to load elements in chunks of K, sort each chunk, then merge with topk. But what about the last chunk which might be smaller than K? I can just load the remaining elements and fill the rest with -inf. Actually, an even simpler approach: process elements one at a time but use a more efficient insertion. That's way too much. GPU limit is 255 per thread. This will spill badly. I need to be more register-efficient. This is tight but might fit. For K=32: 128 registers. Very comfortable. For K=16: 64 registers. For K=8: 32 registers. For sequential merge, the cost per chunk is: - Sort chunk of size C: bitonic sort = C * log2(C)^2 / 2 comparators - Merge with topk: K comparisons If C = K, sort cost = K * log2(K)^2 / 2, merge cost = K. Total per chunk: K * (log2(K)^2 / 2 + 1). For n elements, number of chunks = n / K. Total cost: n * (log2(K)^2 / 2 + 1). For K=64: log2(64) = 6, so cost per element = 6^2/2 + 1 = 19 comparisons. For n=1024: 1024 * 19 = 19456 comparisons. vs. simple insertion: 1024 * 64 = 65536 comparisons. Improvement: 3.4x. For n=512: 512 * 19 = 9728 comparisons. At 1.5GHz, ~6.5 microseconds. Much better! Take top-k For n/thread = 512, sorting 512 elements is 512 * 9 * 9 / 2 = 20736 comparisons. Then take top-64. This is actually more comparisons than insertion sort (32768 vs 20736)! What if we use a partial sort? We only need top-64 out of 512. A partial heap or tournament tree would be O(512 * log(64)) = 512 * 6 = 3072 operations. That's much better! A tournament tree / heap of size 64: - Insert 512 elements one by one - Each insertion: compare with root (smallest in heap), if larger, replace root and sift down - Sift down: at most log2(64) = 6 comparisons Total: 512 * 6 = 3072 comparisons. vs. 32768 for insertion sort. That's 10x better! But a heap in registers is tricky. The sift-down requires accessing elements at indices that depend on the tree structure. In a binary heap stored in an array: - Root at 0 - Children of i at 2i+1, 2i+2 - Parent of i at (i-1)/2 For sift-down: ```cuda int pos = 0; while (2*pos+1 < K) { int child = 2*pos+1; if (child+1 < K && vals[child+1] > vals[child]) child++; if (vals[pos] >= vals[child]) break; swap(vals[pos], vals[child]); swap(idxs[pos], idxs[child]); pos = child; } ``` This is up to log2(K) = 6 swaps, but each swap is 3 assignments, and there are branches. For K=64 and 512 elements: 512 * 6 * (some constant) = ~3000 operations. But wait, for heap-based top-k, we actually want a MIN-heap of the current top-k. The root is the smallest of the top-k. When we see a new element: - If new element > root, replace root and sift-down - If new element <= root, ignore After processing all elements, the heap contains the top-k but not sorted. We need to extract them in sorted order. We can do this by repeatedly extracting the max... but a min-heap doesn't support efficient max extraction. Alternatively, after building the min-heap of top-k, we can sort the heap array using a sort (64 * 6 * 6 / 2 = 1152 comparisons) to get them in descending order. Total: 512 * 6 + 1152 = 4224 operations for heap + sort. vs. insertion: 32768. That's 7.8x better! And register usage: just the heap array of 64 + temp variables. Very efficient! Let me implement the heap approach. ```cuda template <int K> __device__ void heap_sift_down(float* vals, int* idxs, int pos) { while (true) { int left = 2 * pos + 1; int right = 2 * pos + 2; int smallest = pos; if (left < K && vals[left] < vals[smallest]) smallest = left; if (right < K && vals[right] < vals[smallest]) smallest = right; if (smallest == pos) break; float tmp_v = vals[pos]; vals[pos] = vals[smallest]; vals[smallest] = tmp_v; int tmp_i = idxs[pos]; idxs[pos] = idxs[smallest]; idxs[smallest] = tmp_i; pos = smallest; } } template <int K> __device__ void build_min_heap(float* vals, int* idxs) { for (int i = K / 2 - 1; i >= 0; i--) { heap_sift_down<K>(vals, idxs, i); } } template <int K> __device__ void heap_sort_descending(float* vals, int* idxs) { // Convert min-heap to sorted descending for (int i = K - 1; i > 0; i--) { // Move max (actually, for min-heap, the max is somewhere in the leaves) // Hmm, heap sort from min-heap gives ascending order, not descending. // We need max-heap for descending order. } } ``` Wait, I want the final output sorted descending. If I use a min-heap, at the end I have the top-k elements but unsorted. To get descending order: - Build a max-heap instead? No, for streaming top-k we need a min-heap to quickly check if a new element belongs. - After processing, we can just sort the K elements using bitonic sort. So: 1. Initialize heap with -inf 2. For each element: if > root, replace root and sift-down 3. After all elements, sort the K elements descending For step 2, the heap is a min-heap (root is smallest of top-k). For step 3, bitonic sort of K elements descending. This is clean! Let me write it. ```cuda template <int K> __device__ void heap_replace_root(float* vals, int* idxs, float new_val, int new_idx) { vals[0] = new_val; idxs[0] = new_idx; int pos = 0; while (true) { int left = 2 * pos + 1; int right = 2 * pos + 2; int smallest = pos; if (left < K && vals[left] < vals[smallest]) smallest = left; if (right < K && vals[right] < vals[smallest]) smallest = right; if (smallest == pos) break; float tmp_v = vals[pos]; vals[pos] = vals[smallest]; vals[smallest] = tmp_v; int tmp_i = idxs[pos]; idxs[pos] = idxs[smallest]; idxs[smallest] = tmp_i; pos = smallest; } } template <int K> __device__ void bitonic_sort_desc(float* vals, int* idxs, int n) { for (int k = 2; k <= n; k *= 2) { for (int j = k / 2; j > 0; j /= 2) { for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { bool dir = (i & k) == 0; if (dir) { if (vals[i] < vals[ixj]) { float tv = vals[i]; vals[i] = vals[ixj]; vals[ixj] = tv; int ti = idxs[i]; idxs[i] = idxs[ixj]; idxs[ixj] = ti; } } else { if (vals[i] > vals[ixj]) { float tv = vals[i]; vals[i] = vals[ixj]; vals[ixj] = tv; int ti = idxs[i]; idxs[i] = idxs[ixj]; idxs[ixj] = ti; } } } } } } } ``` Now the thread-level processing: ```cuda float vals[K]; int idxs[K]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { vals[i] = -INFINITY; idxs[i] = -1; } // Optional: build initial heap with first K elements // But since we stream, we can just process all elements for (int i = tid; i < n; i += blockDim.x) { float v = row_x[i]; if (v > vals[0]) { heap_replace_root<K>(vals, idxs, v, i); } } // Sort descending bitonic_sort_desc<K>(vals, idxs, K); ``` For K=64, 512 elements per thread: - Heap operations: 512 sift-downs, each up to 6 levels - Each level: 2 comparisons + 3 swaps (9 ops) - Total: 512 * 6 * 9 = 27648 operations - Plus sort: 1152 comparisons - Total: ~28800 operations Hmm, that's actually similar to insertion sort (32768). The heap has less comparison but more memory movement (swaps). In registers, swaps are fast but still take instructions. Wait, I can optimize the heap by not doing full swaps. Instead, track the position and do a single assignment at the end: ```cuda template <int K> __device__ void heap_replace_root_fast(float* vals, int* idxs, float new_val, int new_idx) { int pos = 0; while (true) { int left = 2 * pos + 1; int right = 2 * pos + 2; int smallest = pos; if (left < K && vals[left] < vals[smallest]) smallest = left; if (right < K && vals[right] < vals[smallest]) smallest = right; if (smallest == pos) break; pos = smallest; } // Shift elements along the path and insert at pos // Wait, we need to shift from root to pos, but in a heap the parent is always <= children. // Actually, if we just put new_val at root and sift down, we compare with children and move // the smaller child up. But in our case, we want to maintain min-heap property. // Standard sift down: // Start at root with new_val // While current node > smaller child: // Move smaller child up // Go to that child position // Insert new_val at final position } ``` Ah, the standard sift-down is: ```cuda float v = new_val; int idx = new_idx; int pos = 0; while (true) { int left = 2 * pos + 1; int right = 2 * pos + 2; int smallest = pos; if (left < K && vals[left] < v) smallest = left; if (right < K && vals[right] < v) smallest = right; if (smallest == pos) { vals[pos] = v; idxs[pos] = idx; break; } vals[pos] = vals[smallest]; idxs[pos] = idxs[smallest]; pos = smallest; } ``` This does at most log2(K) iterations, each with 2 comparisons and 2 assignments (moving child up). Then 2 assignments at the end. Total: 6 * (2+2) + 2 = 26 operations per sift-down. For 512 elements: 512 * 26 = 13312 operations. Plus sort: 1152. Total: ~14500. vs. insertion sort: 512 * 64 = 32768 comparisons + 32768 shifts = ~65536 operations. Heap approach: ~14500 operations. 4.5x better! And register usage: just vals[K] and idxs[K]. No extra temp arrays needed for sift-down (just a few scalar variables). This is excellent! Let me use the heap approach. Wait, there's a subtle issue. The heap requires `vals[0]` to be the smallest of the top-k. But initially, all vals are -inf. After replacing root with the first K elements, the heap property might not hold. Actually, if we start with all -inf, and we replace root with element 0, then root is still -inf (if element 0 is -inf, which it isn't... wait, element 0 is some value > -inf). Let me trace: initial vals = [-inf, -inf, ..., -inf]. Process element 0 (v=1.0): v > vals[0] (-inf), replace root with 1.0, sift down. Since children are -inf, 1.0 stays at root. vals = [1.0, -inf, ..., -inf]. Process element 1 (v=2.0): v > vals[0] (1.0), replace root with 2.0, sift down. vals = [2.0, -inf, ..., -inf]. ... Process element K-1 (v=K): vals = [K, -inf, ..., -inf]. At this point, the heap property is violated! The root is K, but many children are -inf. A min-heap requires parent <= children. Here parent > children. But wait, the sift-down algorithm I wrote compares the new value with children and moves smaller children up. If children are -inf, they'll move up, and the new value will go down. Let me trace more carefully: - vals = [1.0, -inf, -inf, ...] - Replace root with 2.0, sift down: - pos=0, left=1 (-inf), right=2 (-inf). smallest=1 (or 2). Move vals[1] (-inf) to pos 0. pos=1. - pos=1, left=3 (-inf), right=4 (-inf). Move vals[3] to pos 1. pos=3. - ... continue down to a leaf. - Insert 2.0 at leaf. After this, the heap is almost all -inf except one leaf has 2.0. That's not right either. Hmm, I see the problem. In a min-heap, when we replace the root (minimum), we should put the new element at the root and sift it down to maintain the heap property. But in our case, the new element is LARGER than the root (that's why we're replacing). So it will sift down until it finds a position where it's <= its children. Since many children might be -inf, it might go all the way to a leaf. This means after processing the first few elements, the large elements end up at the leaves, and -inf values bubble up to the root. Then subsequent elements are compared with -inf at the root, so almost all of them replace the root and sift down. This is actually correct behavior! After processing all elements, the heap will contain the K largest elements, with the smallest of those K at the root. The large elements will be scattered throughout the heap. Wait, let me verify. After processing many elements: - The root should be the K-th largest (smallest among top-K) - All elements in the heap should be >= root - This is exactly the min-heap property! Yes, this works. When we replace root with a new element that's larger, it sifts down to its correct position. When we replace with an element that's smaller, it might not sift down at all and stay at root. Actually, in our algorithm, we only replace if new element > root. So the new element is always > current root. It will sift down to some position. The old root is discarded. After processing all elements, the heap contains the K largest elements seen so far, with the smallest of them at the root. Perfect! Then we sort the heap in descending order using bitonic sort. One more thing: the sift-down algorithm I wrote compares `v` with children, not `vals[pos]` with children. This is the standard sift-down where we start with a "hole" at the root and move it down: ```cuda float v = new_val; int idx = new_idx; int pos = 0; while (true) { int left = 2 * pos + 1; int right = 2 * pos + 2; int smallest = pos; if (left < K && vals[left] < v) smallest = left; if (right < K && vals[right] < v) smallest = right; if (smallest == pos) break; vals[pos] = vals[smallest]; idxs[pos] = idxs[smallest]; pos = smallest; } vals[pos] = v; idxs[pos] = idx; ``` Yes! This is correct and efficient. Now, what about when there are duplicate values or ties? The heap handles this fine. The final sort is stable enough for our purposes (we don't care about tie-breaking for indices). Great, I think the heap approach is the way to go. Let me now design the full kernel with warp-level and block-level merging. Actually, wait. I was going to do warp merge + block merge. But with the heap approach, each thread processes its chunk and produces a sorted top-k. Then we still need to merge. For warp merge, I can use the same heap approach within the warp! Each thread has sorted top-k. We want to merge 32 sorted top-k lists into one top-k list. One approach: 1. Write all 32*k candidates to shared memory 2. For block-level merge of warp results, also use bitonic sort. OK here's the final kernel design: **Kernel: `topk_kernel<K, THREADS>`** Parameters: - `K`: compile-time k - `THREADS`: threads per block (128 or 256) Shared memory layout: - Warp work area: `num_warps * 32 * K * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int))` - Block candidate area: `num_warps * K * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int))` Thread processing: 1. Initialize heap with -inf 2. Strided loop over elements, heap replace if larger 3. Bitonic sort thread's top-k in registers 4. Write to warp shared memory 5. Warp-level bitonic sort of 32*K candidates 6. Warp's thread 0 writes top-k to block candidate area 7. __syncthreads() 8. Block-level bitonic sort of num_warps*K candidates 9. Write top-k to output Wait, in step 6, I said "warp's thread 0 writes top-k". But we can have all threads in the warp participate. After the warp-level sort, top-k are at indices 0..K-1 in warp shared memory. I can have all threads in the block participate in writing to the block candidate area. Actually, since each warp sorts independently in its own shared memory region, after sort the top-k are at the start of each warp's region. Then I need to copy them to the block candidate area. Let me use a dedicated step: - After warp sort, if (lane < K), write `my_warp_vals[lane]` to `s_block_vals[warp_id * K + lane]` This way, all warps write simultaneously and no thread is idle. Then `__syncthreads()` and block sort. For the block sort, all threads participate. After sort, threads 0..K-1 write to output. This is clean! Now let me think about the bitonic sort in shared memory for the warp level. With 32 threads in a warp, each thread can process multiple elements. For warp_cand = 32 * K: - Number of threads = 32 - Elements per thread = K In each stage, thread `lane` processes elements `lane, lane+32, lane+64, ...` up to `warp_cand`. For K=64, warp_cand = 2048. Each thread processes 64 elements. In each `__syncwarp()`, the threads cooperate on the sort. For j=1: thread lane compares `lane*64 + i` and `lane*64 + i ^ 1` for various i. Wait, the indexing is `i` and `i ^ j`, not `lane*64 + i`. The standard loop is: ```cuda for (int i = lane; i < warp_cand; i += 32) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { // compare and swap } } ``` For j=1: i=lane, lane+32, lane+64, ... ixj = i ^ 1. For even i, ixj = i+1. For odd i, ixj = i-1. So thread lane compares pairs (i, i^1) where i = lane, lane+32, etc. For even lane: compares (lane, lane+1), (lane+32, lane+33), etc. For odd lane: compares (lane-1, lane), (lane+31, lane+32), etc. But the `ixj > i` check means odd lanes skip. So only even lanes do the compare-and-swap. That's fine - half the threads are active. For j=2: i = lane, lane+32, ... ixj = i ^ 2. For lane=0: compares (0,2), (32,34), etc. For lane=1: (1,3), (33,35), etc. For lane=2: ixj=0, but 0 < 2, so skip. For lane=3: ixj=1, 1 < 3, skip. So lanes 0 and 1 are active, lanes 2 and 3 skip. This pattern continues. In each stage, half the threads are active. This is actually quite efficient. But there's still the bank conflict issue. For large j (like j=1024), thread lane accesses i=lane and ixj=lane^1024. Both map to the same bank if 1024 is a multiple of the number of banks. With 32-byte banks (8 floats per bank), 1024 / 8 = 128, so i and i^1024 are in the same bank. But different threads access different pairs, so there might not be conflicts within a warp. Wait, for j=1024 and lane=0: accesses 0 and 1024. Bank(0) = 0, Bank(1024) = 1024 % 8 = 0. Same bank! For lane=1: accesses 1 and 1025. Bank(1) = 1, Bank(1025) = 1. Same bank! For lane=2: accesses 2 and 1026. Bank(2) = 2, Bank(1026) = 2. Same bank! So ALL threads in the warp access two addresses in the SAME bank. That's a 32-way bank conflict! But wait, modern GPUs (since Volta) have memory access multicast/broadcast. If all threads in a warp access the same bank but different addresses, it's serialized. If they access the same address, it's broadcast. For j=1024, each thread accesses two DIFFERENT addresses in the same bank. So the warp needs 2 * 32 = 64 accesses to that bank, serialized into 64 transactions. That's terrible! However, the bank width is 4 bytes on older GPUs and 8 bytes on newer ones. Let me check Blackwell... I think it's 8 bytes (2 floats per bank) or maybe 32 bytes. Actually, for shared memory banks: - Pre-Volta: 4 bytes per bank, 32 banks - Volta+: 4 bytes per bank, 32 banks (but independent thread scheduling) - Some sources say Ampere/Hopper have 32-byte banks for certain access patterns Actually, I think for NVIDIA GPUs, shared memory has 32 banks, each 4 bytes wide. So consecutive 4-byte words are in consecutive banks. Two addresses in the same bank if they differ by a multiple of 128 bytes (32 * 4). For j=1024, addresses are i*4 and (i^1024)*4 bytes. The difference is 1024*4 = 4096 bytes, which is a multiple of 128. So yes, same bank. For j=512: diff = 512*4 = 2048, multiple of 128. Same bank. For j=256: diff = 1024, multiple of 128. Same bank. For j=128: diff = 512, multiple of 128. Same bank. For j=64: diff = 256, not multiple of 128. Different banks! For j=32: diff = 128, multiple of 128. Same bank. Wait, 32*4 = 128. Yes, j=32 also causes same bank. So for j = 32, 64, 128, 256, 512, 1024, most of these cause same-bank accesses! That's many stages with bank conflicts. To fix this, we can use padded indexing. Instead of `s[i]`, use `s[i + (i >> 5)]` or similar. But then `i^j` needs to map correctly too. A common padding is: ```cuda #define IDX(i) ((i) + ((i) >> 5)) ``` This adds one extra element every 32 elements, shifting elements 32+ apart by 1. So `IDX(0)=0, IDX(1)=1, ..., IDX(31)=31, IDX(32)=33, IDX(33)=34, ...`. Now, for j=32: i and i^32. - i=0: IDX(0)=0, IDX(32)=33. Banks: 0 % 32 = 0, 33 % 32 = 1. Different! - i=1: IDX(1)=1, IDX(33)=34. Banks: 1, 2. Different! For j=64: i=0: IDX(0)=0, IDX(64)=66. Banks: 0, 2. Different! i=32: IDX(32)=33, IDX(96)=99. Banks: 1, 3. Different! For j=1024: i=0: IDX(0)=0, IDX(1024)=1056. Banks: 0, 1056 % 32 = 0. Still same bank! Hmm, j=1024 corresponds to offset of 1024 elements = 4096 bytes. Even with padding, 4096 / 128 = 32 padding slots inserted, so the offset in memory is 4096 + 32 * something. Actually, for i=0: IDX(0)=0. For i=1024: there are 1024/32 = 32 padding slots inserted before index 1024. So IDX(1024) = 1024 + 32 = 1056. The byte offset is 1056 * 4 = 4224. 4224 / 128 = 33. So bank = 4224 % 128 / 4 = ... wait, bank = (byte_offset / 4) % 32 = 1056 % 32 = 0. Same as IDX(0) = 0 % 32 = 0. So j=1024 still conflicts. But j=1024 is large, and there are only log2(N) - 10 stages with j >= 1024 (for N=2048, only j=1024). For N=2048, the stages with j=1024 is just one stage. The conflict is 2-way (each thread accesses 2 addresses in the same bank), so 2 transactions per thread. With 32 threads, that's 64 transactions total. It's bad but only for one stage. For j=512: i=0, IDX(0)=0, IDX(512)=512+16=528. Bank: 528 % 32 = 16. Different from 0! Great. For j=256: i=0, IDX(0)=0, IDX(256)=256+8=264. Bank: 264 % 32 = 8. Different! For j=128: i=0, IDX(0)=0, IDX(128)=128+4=132. Bank: 132 % 32 = 4. Different! For j=64: i=0, IDX(0)=0, IDX(64)=64+2=66. Bank: 66 % 32 = 2. Different! For j=32: i=0, IDX(0)=0, IDX(32)=32+1=33. Bank: 33 % 32 = 1. Different! So with `IDX(i) = i + (i >> 5)`, the only remaining conflict is j=1024 (and j=2048, etc., powers of 2 >= 1024). For N <= 2048, only j=1024 conflicts. For N=16384, j=8192, 16384 would also conflict. This is a huge improvement! Let me use this padding. The total shared memory size with padding: for N elements, we need N + N/32 elements. For 2048: 2048 + 64 = 2112. For 16384: 16384 + 512 = 16896. Wait, for the warp-level sort, warp_cand = 32 * K. For K=64, that's 2048. With padding: 2112 floats + 2112 ints = 16.5KB + 16.5KB = 33KB per warp. For 8 warps: 264KB. That's too much! Actually, I miscalculated. Each warp uses its own shared memory region. The regions don't overlap in time (all warps run concurrently). So total shared memory = max per warp * num_warps = 33KB * 8 = 264KB if they're all active simultaneously. But wait, shared memory is per block, and all warps in the block share it. So I need 264KB. That's way too much. I can't use this much shared memory. Let me reconsider. Options: 1. Don't use padding, accept bank conflicts 2. Use fewer warps (fewer threads) 3. Process warp merges sequentially instead of in parallel For option 3: have one warp do all the warp merges one at a time. But then we're not using the full block parallelism. For option 2: use 128 threads (4 warps). Shared memory: 4 * 33KB = 132KB. Still a lot but maybe fits on Blackwell. For option 1: no padding. Let me see how bad the conflicts really are. With j=1024, each thread accesses two addresses in the same bank. On modern GPUs, shared memory can handle 2-way conflicts at half bandwidth. For 32 threads accessing the same bank, it's serialized 64-way. Wait, each thread accesses 2 addresses in the SAME bank, but different addresses. For 32 threads, that's 64 different addresses in one bank. This requires 64 separate transactions. At 1 transaction per clock, that's 64 clocks = ~43 nanoseconds. For one stage. There are log2(N) stages total, so this is a small fraction. Actually, for a warp-level sort of 2048 elements, there are log2(2048) * (log2(2048)+1) / 2 = 11 * 12 / 2 = 66 stages. Each stage has some threads active. The total time is dominated by the many small-j stages where all threads are active and there are no bank conflicts. The large-j stages with conflicts are few in number. So maybe bank conflicts aren't that bad. Let me just use un-padded shared memory and see. Actually, let me think about this from a different angle. For the warp-level sort, with 32 threads and 2048 elements: - j=1: 1024 comparators, 32 threads active -> 32 iterations, no conflicts - j=2: 1024 comparators, 16 threads active -> 16 iterations, some conflicts - ... - j=1024: 1024 comparators, 1 thread active -> 1 iteration, conflict The total "work" in terms of comparator executions is 2048 * 11 * 12 / 4 = 67584 comparators. At 1 comparator per thread per clock, with 32 threads, that's ~2112 clocks = 1.4 microseconds. Even with some slowdown from bank conflicts, it's still very fast. Similarly for block-level sort of 512 elements with 128 threads: - 512 * 9 * 10 / 4 = 11520 comparators - With 128 threads, ~90 clocks = 60 nanoseconds. Very fast! So the sorting time is negligible compared to the heap processing time (~10+ microseconds). Bank conflicts in shared memory sort are not the bottleneck. Each thread can load float4 (4 floats at a time) from global memory. This is especially beneficial for large n. For n=131072 and 128 threads, each thread reads 1024 elements = 256 float4 loads. For n=8192 and 256 threads, each thread reads 32 elements = 8 float4 loads. For n=4096 and 256 threads, each thread reads 16 elements = 4 float4 loads. Vectorized loads can significantly improve memory bandwidth. Actually, one more optimization: for the heap, I can unroll the sift-down loop since K is a template parameter and the max depth is log2(K). For K=64, max depth is 6. I can write the sift-down with a `#pragma unroll` on the loop. ```cuda template <int K> __device__ inline void heap_replace(float* vals, int* idxs, float new_val, int new_idx) { float v = new_val; int idx = new_idx; int pos = 0; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < 6; d++) { // log2(64) = 6 int left = 2 * pos + 1; int right = 2 * pos + 2; int smallest = pos; if (left < K && vals[left] < v) smallest = left; if (right < K && vals[right] < v) smallest = right; if (smallest == pos) { vals[pos] = v; idxs[pos] = idx; return; } vals[pos] = vals[smallest]; idxs[pos] = idxs[smallest]; pos = smallest; } vals[pos] = v; idxs[pos] = idx; } ``` But the compiler might not like the early return inside a loop that we want unrolled. Actually, for a cleaner implementation, let me just write the while loop. For K=64, it iterates at most 6 times. The compiler should inline and unroll it nicely. ```cuda template <int K> __device__ inline void heap_replace(float* vals, int* idxs, float new_val, int new_idx) { float v = new_val; int idx = new_idx; int pos = 0; while (true) { int left = 2 * pos + 1; int right = 2 * pos + 2; int smallest = pos; if (left < K && vals[left] < v) smallest = left; if (right < K && vals[right] < v) smallest = right; if (smallest == pos) break; vals[pos] = vals[smallest]; idxs[pos] = idxs[smallest]; pos = smallest; } vals[pos] = v; idxs[pos] = idx; } ``` This is clean and the compiler should handle it well. Similarly for the bitonic sort in registers: ```cuda template <int K> __device__ inline void bitonic_sort_regs(float* vals, int* idxs) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 2; k <= K; k *= 2) { #pragma unroll for (int j = k / 2; j > 0; j /= 2) { #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { bool dir = (i & k) == 0; if (dir) { if (vals[i] < vals[ixj]) { float tv = vals[i]; vals[i] = vals[ixj]; vals[ixj] = tv; int ti = idxs[i]; idxs[i] = idxs[ixj]; idxs[ixj] = ti; } } else { if (vals[i] > vals[ixj]) { float tv = vals[i]; vals[i] = vals[ixj]; vals[ixj] = tv; int ti = idxs[i]; idxs[i] = idxs[ixj]; idxs[ixj] = ti; } } } } } } } ``` Since K is a template parameter, all loops should be fully unrolled by the compiler.
