Good. Now, let me think about the `topk1_kernel`. I can use 256 threads with float4 loads. ```cuda __global__ void topk1_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out_vals, int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs, int n) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; const float* row_x = x + row * n; float max_val = -INFINITY; int max_idx = -1; // Vectorized load int n4 = n >> 2; const float4* x4 = (const float4*)row_x; for (int i = tid; i < n4; i += blockDim.x) { float4 v4 = x4[i]; if (v4.x > max_val) { max_val = v4.x; max_idx = (i << 2); } if (v4.y > max_val) { max_val = v4.y; max_idx = (i << 2) + 1; } if (v4.z > max_val) { max_val = v4.z; max_idx = (i << 2) + 2; } if (v4.w > max_val) { max_val = v4.w; max_idx = (i << 2) + 3; } } // Handle remaining (none in our case, but for completeness) for (int i = (tid + (n4 << 2)); i < n; i += blockDim.x) { float v = row_x[i]; if (v > max_val) { max_val = v; max_idx = i; } } // Warp reduction unsigned mask = 0xFFFFFFFF; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { float other_val = __shfl_down_sync(mask, max_val, offset); int other_idx = __shfl_down_sync(mask, max_idx, offset); if (other_val > max_val) { max_val = other_val; max_idx = other_idx; } } __shared__ float s_vals[32]; __shared__ int s_idxs[32]; int lane = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; if (lane == 0) { s_vals[warp_id] = max_val; s_idxs[warp_id] = max_idx; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { float v = (tid < ((blockDim.x + 31) >> 5)) ? s_vals[tid] : -INFINITY; int idx = (tid < ((blockDim.x + 31) >> 5)) ? s_idxs[tid] : -1; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { float other_val = __shfl_down_sync(mask, v, offset); int other_idx = __shfl_down_sync(mask, idx, offset); if (other_val > v) { v = other_val; idx = other_idx; } } if (tid == 0) { out_vals[row] = v; out_idxs[row] = idx; } } } ``` This looks good. Now the main kernel template. ```cuda template <int K> __global__ void topk_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out_vals, int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs, int n) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; int num_warps = blockDim.x >> 5; const float* row_x = x + row * n; // Thread-local top-K using min-heap float vals[K]; int idxs[K]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { vals[i] = -INFINITY; idxs[i] = -1; } // Vectorized processing int n4 = n >> 2; const float4* x4 = (const float4*)row_x; for (int i = tid; i < n4; i += blockDim.x) { float4 v4 = x4[i]; if (v4.x > vals[0]) heap_replace<K>(vals, idxs, v4.x, i << 2); if (v4.y > vals[0]) heap_replace<K>(vals, idxs, v4.y, (i << 2) + 1); if (v4.z > vals[0]) heap_replace<K>(vals, idxs, v4.z, (i << 2) + 2); if (v4.w > vals[0]) heap_replace<K>(vals, idxs, v4.w, (i << 2) + 3); } for (int i = (n4 << 2) + tid; i < n; i += blockDim.x) { float v = row_x[i]; if (v > vals[0]) heap_replace<K>(vals, idxs, v, i); } // Sort thread's top-K descending bitonic_sort_regs<K>(vals, idxs); // Shared memory layout // Warp work areas + block candidate area extern __shared__ char smem[]; int warp_cand = 32 * K; int block_cand = num_warps * K; float* s_warp_vals = (float*)smem; int* s_warp_idxs = (int*)(smem + num_warps * warp_cand * sizeof(float)); float* my_warp_vals = s_warp_vals + warp_id * warp_cand; int* my_warp_idxs = s_warp_idxs + warp_id * warp_cand; // Write to warp shared memory #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { my_warp_vals[lane * K + i] = vals[i]; my_warp_idxs[lane * K + i] = idxs[i]; } __syncwarp(); // Bitonic sort within warp for (int kk = 2; kk <= warp_cand; kk <<= 1) { for (int j = kk >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) { for (int i = lane; i < warp_cand; i += 32) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { if ((i & kk) == 0) { if (my_warp_vals[i] < my_warp_vals[ixj]) { float tv = my_warp_vals[i]; my_warp_vals[i] = my_warp_vals[ixj]; my_warp_vals[ixj] = tv; int ti = my_warp_idxs[i]; my_warp_idxs[i] = my_warp_idxs[ixj]; my_warp_idxs[ixj] = ti; } } else { if (my_warp_vals[i] > my_warp_vals[ixj]) { float tv = my_warp_vals[i]; my_warp_vals[i] = my_warp_vals[ixj]; my_warp_vals[ixj] = tv; int ti = my_warp_idxs[i]; my_warp_idxs[i] = my_warp_idxs[ixj]; my_warp_idxs[ixj] = ti; } } } } __syncwarp(); } } // Copy warp top-K to block candidate area float* s_block_vals = (float*)(smem + num_warps * warp_cand * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int))); int* s_block_idxs = (int*)(s_block_vals + block_cand); if (lane < K) { s_block_vals[warp_id * K + lane] = my_warp_vals[lane]; s_block_idxs[warp_id * K + lane] = my_warp_idxs[lane]; } __syncthreads(); // Block-level bitonic sort for (int kk = 2; kk <= block_cand; kk <<= 1) { for (int j = kk >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) { for (int i = tid; i < block_cand; i += blockDim.x) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { if ((i & kk) == 0) { if (s_block_vals[i] < s_block_vals[ixj]) { float tv = s_block_vals[i]; s_block_vals[i] = s_block_vals[ixj]; s_block_vals[ixj] = tv; int ti = s_block_idxs[i]; s_block_idxs[i] = s_block_idxs[ixj]; s_block_idxs[ixj] = ti; } } else { if (s_block_vals[i] > s_block_vals[ixj]) { float tv = s_block_vals[i]; s_block_vals[i] = s_block_vals[ixj]; s_block_vals[ixj] = tv; int ti = s_block_idxs[i]; s_block_idxs[i] = s_block_idxs[ixj]; s_block_idxs[ixj] = ti; } } } } __syncthreads(); } } // Write output if (tid < K) { out_vals[row * K + tid] = s_block_vals[tid]; out_idxs[row * K + tid] = (int64_t)s_block_idxs[tid]; } } ``` Wait, there's a potential issue with shared memory layout. The `extern __shared__ char smem[]` means I need to specify the shared memory size at kernel launch. The size is: `num_warps * warp_cand * sizeof(float) + num_warps * warp_cand * sizeof(int) + block_cand * sizeof(float) + block_cand * sizeof(int)` For K=64, num_warps=4, warp_cand=2048, block_cand=256: - Warp vals: 4 * 2048 * 4 = 32768 - Warp idxs: 4 * 2048 * 4 = 32768 - Block vals: 256 * 4 = 1024 - Block idxs: 256 * 4 = 1024 - Total: 67584 bytes For K=32, num_warps=8, warp_cand=1024, block_cand=256: - Warp vals: 8 * 1024 * 4 = 32768 - Warp idxs: 8 * 1024 * 4 = 32768 - Block vals: 256 * 4 = 1024 - Block idxs: 256 * 4 = 1024 - Total: 67584 bytes Interesting, both use the same total shared memory! For K=16, num_warps=8, warp_cand=512, block_cand=128: - Warp vals: 8 * 512 * 4 = 16384 - Warp idxs: 8 * 512 * 4 = 16384 - Block vals: 128 * 4 = 512 - Block idxs: 128 * 4 = 512 - Total: 33792 bytes For K=8, num_warps=8, warp_cand=256, block_cand=64: - Warp vals: 8 * 256 * 4 = 8192 - Warp idxs: 8 * 256 * 4 = 8192 - Block vals: 64 * 4 = 256 - Block idxs: 64 * 4 = 256 - Total: 16896 bytes All well within limits. For the warp-level bitonic sort, instead of each thread handling elements `lane, lane+32, lane+64, ...`, I can have each thread handle a contiguous chunk. This might improve shared memory locality. Actually, the standard strided approach is: ```cuda for (int i = lane; i < warp_cand; i += 32) { ... } ``` With 32 threads and warp_cand=2048, each thread handles 64 elements at stride 32. This means thread 0 handles 0, 32, 64, ... and thread 1 handles 1, 33, 65, ... In bitonic sort, when j is small (1, 2, 4, etc.), elements i and i^j are close together. With the strided layout, thread 0 handles both i and i^j for many cases, which means no inter-thread communication is needed! That's great! For example, j=1: i and i^1 are adjacent. Wait, but in the loop, each thread only processes its own i values. It doesn't process ixj. So thread 0 reads s[0] and s[1], compares, and swaps if needed. Thread 0 reads s[32] and s[33], compares, swaps. Thread 1 does nothing for i=33. So each pair (i, i^j) is handled by the thread with the smaller i. If lane is odd, all are odd, so none are processed (ixj = i^1 is even < odd i). So for j=1, only even lanes do work! Lanes 0, 2, 4, ... are active. 16 threads do work, 16 are idle. This is actually fine - GPU warps can handle this with some threads masked off. The advantage of this approach is that each active thread reads two elements that are close together in memory (for small j). For j=1, they're adjacent. For j=2, they're 2 apart. This is cache-friendly and has minimal bank conflicts (for j < 32, they're in different banks!). For large j, like j=1024, elements are far apart. Let me just trust the compiler with the while loop. Actually, I just realized: for K=8, 16, 32, the heap_replace with a while loop that iterates at most log2(K) times should be very fast. The compiler will likely unroll it since the loop bound is small and K is a compile-time constant... well, K is a template parameter, so the compiler knows it. But the while loop exit depends on runtime data (the heap structure), so the compiler might not unroll it fully. I can use `#pragma unroll` on the while loop if I convert it to a for loop with a fixed upper bound: ```cuda template <int K> __device__ inline void heap_replace(float* vals, int* idxs, float new_val, int new_idx) { float v = new_val; int idx = new_idx; int pos = 0; bool done = false; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < 6; d++) { if (!done) { int left = 2 * pos + 1; int right = 2 * pos + 2; int smallest = pos; if (left < K && vals[left] < v) smallest = left; if (right < K && vals[right] < v) smallest = right; if (smallest == pos) { vals[pos] = v; idxs[pos] = idx; done = true; } else { vals[pos] = vals[smallest]; idxs[pos] = idxs[smallest]; pos = smallest; } } } if (!done) { vals[pos] = v; idxs[pos] = idx; } } ``` For K=8, the loop runs 6 times but the first 3 iterations do real work and the rest are masked by `done`. The compiler might optimize away the masked iterations, or it might not. Actually, with `if (!done)` inside a loop, the compiler can see that once `done` is true, the rest of the iterations do nothing. But whether it eliminates them depends on optimization level. Let me just use the while loop with `#pragma unroll`. NVCC supports `#pragma unroll` on while loops too, but you need to specify the count. ```cuda #pragma unroll 6 while (true) { // ... if (smallest == pos) break; // ... } ``` Wait, I don't think `#pragma unroll` works well with `while(true)` that has a `break`. +"""Custom CUDA top-k kernel optimized for Blackwell SM120."""
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline
+
+CUDA_SOURCE = r"""
+#include <torch/extension.h>
+#include <cuda_runtime.h>
+#include <cuda_fp16.h>
+#include <float.h>
+
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+// Device helpers
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+template <int K>
+__device__ __forceinline__ void heap_replace(float* vals, int* idxs,
+                                              float new_val, int new_idx) {
+    float v = new_val;
+    int idx = new_idx;
+    int pos = 0;
+    bool done = false;
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int d = 0; d < 6; ++d) {
+        if (!done) {
+            int left  = (pos << 1) + 1;
+            int right = (pos << 1) + 2;
+            int smallest = pos;
+            if (left < K && vals[left] < v) smallest = left;
+            if (right < K && vals[right] < v) smallest = right;
+            if (smallest == pos) {
+                vals[pos] = v;
+                idxs[pos] = idx;
+                done = true;
+            } else {
+                vals[pos] = vals[smallest];
+                idxs[pos] = idxs[smallest];
+                pos = smallest;
+            }
+        }
+    }
+    if (!done) {
+        vals[pos] = v;
+        idxs[pos] = idx;
+    }
+}
+
+template <int K>
+__device__ __forceinline__ void bitonic_sort_regs(float* vals, int* idxs) {
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int k = 2; k <= K; k <<= 1) {
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int j = k >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) {
+            #pragma unroll
+            for (int i = 0; i < K; ++i) {
+                int ixj = i ^ j;
+                if (ixj > i) {
+                    bool dir = (i & k) == 0;
+                    if (dir) {
+                        if (vals[i] < vals[ixj]) {
+                            float tv = vals[i]; vals[i] = vals[ixj]; vals[ixj] = tv;
+                            int ti = idxs[i]; idxs[i] = idxs[ixj]; idxs[ixj] = ti;
+                        }
+                    } else {
+                        if (vals[i] > vals[ixj]) {
+                            float tv = vals[i]; vals[i] = vals[ixj]; vals[ixj] = tv;
+                            int ti = idxs[i]; idxs[i] = idxs[ixj]; idxs[ixj] = ti;
+                        }
+                    }
+                }
+            }
+        }
+    }
+}
+
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+// k = 1  (argmax) – warp-shuffle reduction, float4 loads
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+__global__ void topk1_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x,
+                             float* __restrict__ out_vals,
+                             int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs,
+                             int n) {
+    int row = blockIdx.x;
+    int tid = threadIdx.x;
+    const float* row_x = x + row * n;
+
+    float max_val = -FLT_MAX;
+    int   max_idx = -1;
+
+    int n4 = n >> 2;
+    const float4* x4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_x);
+    for (int i = tid; i < n4; i += blockDim.x) {
+        float4 v4 = x4[i];
+        if (v4.x > max_val) { max_val = v4.x; max_idx = (i << 2);     }
+        if (v4.y > max_val) { max_val = v4.y; max_idx = (i << 2) + 1; }
+        if (v4.z > max_val) { max_val = v4.z; max_idx = (i << 2) + 2; }
+        if (v4.w > max_val) { max_val = v4.w; max_idx = (i << 2) + 3; }
+    }
+    for (int i = (n4 << 2) + tid; i < n; i += blockDim.x) {
+        float v = row_x[i];
+        if (v > max_val) { max_val = v; max_idx = i; }
+    }
+
+    unsigned mask = 0xFFFFFFFFu;
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {
+        float other_val = __shfl_down_sync(mask, max_val, offset);
+        int   other_idx = __shfl_down_sync(mask, max_idx, offset);
+        if (other_val > max_val) { max_val = other_val; max_idx = other_idx; }
+    }
+
+    __shared__ float s_vals[32];
+    __shared__ int   s_idxs[32];
+    int lane = tid & 31;
+    int warp_id = tid >> 5;
+    if (lane == 0) {
+        s_vals[warp_id] = max_val;
+        s_idxs[warp_id] = max_idx;
+    }
+    __syncthreads();
+
+    if (tid < 32) {
+        float v = (tid < ((blockDim.x + 31) >> 5)) ? s_vals[tid] : -FLT_MAX;
+        int   idx = (tid < ((blockDim.x + 31) >> 5)) ? s_idxs[tid] : -1;
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {
+            float other_val = __shfl_down_sync(mask, v, offset);
+            int   other_idx = __shfl_down_sync(mask, idx, offset);
+            if (other_val > v) { v = other_val; idx = other_idx; }
+        }
+        if (tid == 0) {
+            out_vals[row] = v;
+            out_idxs[row] = idx;
+        }
+    }
+}
+
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+// Generic k > 1
+// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+template <int K>
+__global__ void topk_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x,
+                            float* __restrict__ out_vals,
+                            int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs,
+                            int n) {
+    int row = blockIdx.x;
+    int tid = threadIdx.x;
+    int lane = tid & 31;
+    int warp_id = tid >> 5;
+    int num_warps = blockDim.x >> 5;
+    const float* row_x = x + row * n;
+
+    float vals[K];
+    int   idxs[K];
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int i = 0; i < K; ++i) {
+        vals[i] = -FLT_MAX;
+        idxs[i] = -1;
+    }
+
+    int n4 = n >> 2;
+    const float4* x4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_x);
+    for (int i = tid; i < n4; i += blockDim.x) {
+        float4 v4 = x4[i];
+        if (v4.x > vals[0]) heap_replace<K>(vals, idxs, v4.x, (i << 2));
+        if (v4.y > vals[0]) heap_replace<K>(vals, idxs, v4.y, (i << 2) + 1);
+        if (v4.z > vals[0

... (6301 more chars)

Actually, let me try a different approach: instead of a full bitonic sort within the warp, I can use a simpler merge algorithm. Each thread has a sorted top-k. I want to merge 32 sorted top-k lists into one top-k list. I can use a tournament/tree approach: 1. Load all 32*k candidates into shared memory 2. Use a parallel reduction to repeatedly find the maximum For finding the maximum of N elements with 32 threads: - Tree reduction: 32 threads load 32 elements, warp shuffle to find max, repeat. To get top-k, I'd need to do this k times, each time removing the found max. That's k * log2(N) operations with warp shuffles. For k=64 and N=2048: 64 * 11 = 704 warp shuffle operations. Each shuffle is ~1 clock. So ~704 clocks = 0.5 microseconds. Plus memory loads. Wait, but to "remove" the found max, I need to mark it and ignore it in subsequent iterations. For k=64: 64 * 11 = 704 operations. Very fast! And shared memory is just 32*k elements = 2048 floats + 2048 ints = 16KB per warp. For k=64, each element requires a heap sift-down with up to 6 levels, which is ~20-30 operations. With 1024 elements per thread, that's 20,000-30,000 operations. At 1.5GHz, that's 13-20 microseconds per thread. But with 128 threads, the block still takes that long (they run in parallel but the block has only 128 threads, so one warp at a time on an SM). Actually, 128 threads = 4 warps. An SM can run many warps concurrently. But if the kernel is compute-bound, the time is determined by the operations per thread. Wait, but 4 warps on an SM with 1.5GHz should finish 30,000 operations in about 20 microseconds. My actual time is 1700 microseconds. That's 85x slower! Something is very wrong. Let me think... could it be that the bitonic sort in registers is generating massive code that causes instruction cache thrashing? Or register spilling? For K=64, bitonic_sort_regs generates 672 compare-swap operations, each with branches. The compiler might generate a lot of instructions. But 672 instructions is tiny. Or maybe the issue is that the compiler is NOT unrolling the loops properly, and instead is doing dynamic indexing into the register arrays, which causes register spills to local memory? Ah! That's a very important point. In CUDA, arrays in registers (`float vals[64]`) are typically mapped to registers if the indexing is compile-time constant. But if the compiler can't determine the index at compile time, it will spill to local memory. In `heap_replace`, the index `pos` is dynamic. So `vals[pos]` and `idxs[pos]` access local memory, not registers! Local memory is cached but much slower than registers. Similarly, in `bitonic_sort_regs`, the loops over `i`, `j`, `k` are unrolled, so `vals[i]` and `vals[ixj]` should be compile-time constants. But the compiler might not fully unroll the inner loop if it's too large. Wait, `bitonic_sort_regs` has: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < K; ++i) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { ... vals[i] ... vals[ixj] ... } } ``` For K=64, this loop has 64 iterations. The `#pragma unroll` should fully unroll it. For each iteration, `i` and `ixj` are compile-time constants (since `j` and `k` are also compile-time known after unrolling). So `vals[i]` should map to specific registers. But `vals` is declared as `float vals[K];` which is a local array. The compiler can either allocate registers for each element or spill to local memory. With 64 floats and 64 ints = 128 values per thread, plus other variables, the total register usage might be high but should fit. For K=64 with 128 threads per block, if each thread uses ~150 registers, the block uses 128 * 150 = 19200 registers. An SM has 65536 registers, so this fits easily. But wait, the warp-level bitonic sort uses shared memory. The block-level sort also uses shared memory. These don't use registers. Actually, a more practical approach: let me first run ncu to profile the kernel and see what's taking time. Or I can use `torch.profiler`. But before profiling, let me think about a much simpler and likely faster approach. **Alternative approach: Two-pass with much simpler first pass** Instead of maintaining a heap per thread, just have each thread find its top-1 or top-2 element. Then at the warp/block level, merge all thread results to get top-k. Wait, that doesn't work. If each thread only keeps top-1, and the true top-64 are all in one thread's chunk, we'd miss 63 of them. But what if we use a larger number of threads and have each thread keep fewer elements? For example, with 1024 threads and k=64, each thread processes 128 elements and keeps top-1. Then we have 1024 candidates, from which we need top-64. A bitonic sort of 1024 elements is fast. But 1024 threads per block is a lot. Actually, what about using 512 threads for k=64? - Each thread processes 256 elements, keeps top-1. - 512 candidates, sort to get top-64. - But the problem is that the top-64 might be spread across many threads. If each thread keeps only top-1, we'd only get 1 true top element if all top-64 are in one thread's chunk. So each thread MUST keep at least k elements to guarantee correctness. Hmm, but what if we use a two-pass approach where the first pass uses many threads and each keeps top-k, and the second pass merges? That's what I was doing originally. The issue is the first pass is too slow. Wait, what if we use a block-level heap in shared memory instead of per-thread heaps? All threads in a block cooperatively insert elements into a shared top-k heap. But a shared memory heap would have massive contention. Each insertion requires atomic operations or serialized access. What about a different data structure? Instead of a heap, use a sorted array and binary search for insertion position. For K=64, binary search is 6 comparisons, then shift up to 64 elements. That's 6 + 64 = 70 operations per insertion. Worse than heap. Or use a tournament tree / selection tree in registers? Complex. Could it be that `cudaFuncSetAttribute` is causing some overhead? No, that's a one-time host-side call. Wait, I just realized something. For k=64 with 128 threads: - block_cand = 4 * 64 = 256 - In the block-level bitonic sort, the loop does: ```cuda for (int kk = 2; kk <= block_cand; kk <<= 1) { for (int j = kk >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) { for (int i = tid; i < block_cand; i += blockDim.x) { ``` With blockDim.x = 128 and block_cand = 256: - For kk=2, j=1: i = tid, tid+128. 2 iterations per thread. - For kk=4, j=2,1: 2+2 = 4 iterations per thread. - ... - For kk=256, j=128,64,32,16,8,4,2,1: 8 stages, each with 2 iterations per thread = 16 iterations. Total: about 2+4+6+8+10+12+14+16 = 72 iterations per thread. Each iteration does a compare-and-swap. So ~72 operations per thread. Very fast. The warp-level sort with warp_cand=2048 and 32 threads: - Total stages: log2(2048) * (log2(2048)+1) / 2 = 11 * 12 / 2 = 66 stages. - Each stage: 2048 / 32 = 64 iterations per thread. - Total: 66 * 64 = 4224 iterations per thread. Each with compare-and-swap. - At 1 operation per iteration, ~4224 operations. Fast. The bitonic sort in registers for K=64: - 672 compare-swap operations. Fast. The heap_replace for 1024 elements: - Each call: up to 6 iterations, each with 2 comparisons, 2 assignments. - Say ~20 operations per call. - 1024 calls = ~20480 operations. Something is seriously wrong. Wait... could it be that the compiler is not unrolling the heap_replace loop, and instead of using registers, it's storing `vals` and `idxs` in local memory? Let me think about this. `vals` is declared as `float vals[K];` in the kernel. K=64. With 128 threads, each thread has a 64-element float array. The compiler tries to map this to registers. For `bitonic_sort_regs`, all indices `i` and `ixj` are compile-time constants after unrolling. So the compiler should definitely use registers there. But `vals` is the SAME array as in `heap_replace`. If the compiler decides to spill it for `heap_replace`, it might spill it everywhere. Actually, in PTX/local memory terminology, a local array can be partially in registers and partially in local memory. But typically, if ANY access is dynamic, the compiler might put the whole array in local memory. If `vals[64]` and `idxs[64]` are in local memory, that's 512 bytes per thread. For 128 threads = 64KB local memory per block. Local memory is backed by global memory (L1/L2 cached). Each heap_replace operation would require multiple local memory loads/stores, which are very slow. This would explain the 100x slowdown! To fix this, I need to ensure the compiler keeps `vals` and `idxs` in registers. One trick is to use individual scalar variables instead of arrays: ```cuda float v0, v1, v2, ..., v63; int i0, i1, i2, ..., i63; ``` But this is extremely tedious to write and doesn't scale. Looking at the ptxas output: For `topk_kernel<64>`: - 512 bytes stack frame - 0 bytes spill stores, 0 bytes spill loads - 79 registers - 512 bytes cumulative stack size For `topk_kernel<32>`: - 256 bytes stack frame - 48 registers - 256 bytes cumulative stack size For `topk_kernel<16>`: - 128 bytes stack frame - 48 registers For `topk_kernel<8>`: - 64 bytes stack frame - 39 registers The stack frame size equals `K * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int))` = K * 8 bytes. This means the compiler is storing `vals` and `idxs` in local memory (stack frame), NOT in registers! Even though there are 0 spill stores/loads, the stack frame indicates the arrays are in local memory. Local memory accesses go through the L1 cache, but they're still much slower than registers. This explains the terrible performance! For K=64, every access to `vals[pos]` or `idxs[pos]` in `heap_replace` goes to local memory. With 1024 elements and 6 heap levels per element, that's ~6000 local memory accesses per thread. At L1 latency of ~20-30 cycles, that's ~180,000 cycles = 120 microseconds per thread. But with 128 threads, and cache misses... it could easily be milliseconds. Wait, but ptxas says 0 spill stores/loads. How is that possible if the array is in local memory? Actually, in CUDA terminology, "stack frame" refers to local memory that is explicitly allocated for local arrays, while "spill stores/loads" refer to register spills. The stack frame is where the compiler puts arrays that it decides not to map to registers. It's not counted as "spills" because it's a deliberate allocation. So the compiler is intentionally putting `vals[K]` and `idxs[K]` in local memory because K=64 is too large for register allocation, or because dynamic indexing prevents register mapping. For K=8, the stack frame is 64 bytes. That's `8 * (4 + 4) = 64`. Same pattern. How do I force the compiler to use registers? One approach: use individual variables instead of arrays. If each thread has its own 64-element region in shared memory, and threads access different regions, there are no bank conflicts (assuming proper alignment). For example, thread tid accesses `s_vals[tid * 64 + i]`. The bank is `(tid * 64 + i) % 32`. Since 64 is a multiple of 32, `tid * 64 % 32 = 0`. So thread 0 accesses banks 0..31, thread 1 accesses banks 0..31, etc. ALL threads access the SAME banks! Major conflicts! To avoid this, we need padding: ```cuda s_vals[tid * (64 + 1) + i] // pad by 1 ``` Bank = `(tid * 65 + i) % 32`. For tid=0: banks 0..31. tid=1: banks 1..32%32=0..30,31,0,1. So adjacent threads access shifted banks. Much better! With pad=1, total shared memory = 64 * 65 * 8 = 33.6KB. Still under 48KB. For the heap sift-down in shared memory: ```cuda float v = new_val; int idx = new_idx; int pos = 0; while (true) { int left = 2 * pos + 1; int right = 2 * pos + 2; float left_val = s_warp_vals[tid * 65 + left]; float right_val = s_warp_vals[tid * 65 + right]; int smallest = pos; if (left < K && left_val < v) smallest = left; if (right < K && right_val < v) smallest = right; if (smallest == pos) { s_warp_vals[tid * 65 + pos] = v; s_warp_idxs[tid * 65 + pos] = idx; break; } s_warp_vals[tid * 65 + pos] = s_warp_vals[tid * 65 + smallest]; s_warp_idxs[tid * 65 + pos] = s_warp_idxs[tid * 65 + smallest]; pos = smallest; } ``` This accesses shared memory with dynamic indices. But since each thread accesses its own padded region, there are no inter-thread bank conflicts. Intra-thread accesses might have conflicts if `pos` and `smallest` map to the same bank. Pass 1 simplified: - `batch * num_blocks` blocks, `THREADS` threads each. - Each thread processes chunk_size elements, keeps top-k. - Writes top-k directly to global memory at offset `(batch_idx * num_blocks + block_idx) * THREADS * k + tid * k`. No shared memory needed in pass 1! Just local memory for top-k. Pass 2: For each row, read `num_blocks * THREADS * k` candidates and extract top-k. For n=131072, k=64, THREADS=256: - If num_blocks=4: chunk_size = 131072 / (4*256) = 128 elements per thread. - Candidates = 4 * 256 * 64 = 65536. - Pass 2 needs to find top-64 from 65536 candidates. For pass 2 with 65536 candidates per row: - We can launch 1 block per row with 256 threads. - Load candidates in tiles into shared memory. - Use the tournament tree / repeated max reduction to extract top-64. Wait, loading 65536 candidates is 65536 * 8 = 512KB. That's too much for shared memory. We'd need to process in tiles. Tile-based pass 2: 1. Maintain a top-k array in shared memory (or registers) 2. Load a tile of candidates from global memory into shared memory 3. Merge tile with current top-k 4. Repeat For merging a tile of 1024 candidates with current top-k (64): - Load tile into shared memory (1024 * 8 = 8KB) - Each thread finds its local top-k from the tile - Block-level merge to update global top-k This is getting complex. Let me think of a simpler pass 2. Actually, for pass 2, if we have M candidates and need top-k, and M is large (e.g., 65536), we can: 1. Use a block-level heap in shared memory of size k. 2. Stream through all M candidates in tiles. 3. For each candidate, if it's larger than heap root, replace and sift-down. This is the same heap approach but in shared memory. And with many threads (e.g., 256), each thread can process multiple candidates. For M=65536 and 256 threads: - Each thread processes 256 candidates. - For each candidate, do `if (cand > root) replace_root(heap, cand)`. - But `root` is in shared memory, and `replace_root` requires synchronizing all threads because the heap is shared! We can't have 256 threads independently updating the same heap. That requires atomics or serialization. What if each thread maintains its own local top-k from its subset of candidates, and then we merge all thread results? That's back to the same problem! We'd have 256 * 64 = 16384 candidates to merge. OK, let me try a completely different approach. What if pass 2 uses a **bitonic sort on chunks**? For M=65536 candidates stored in global memory: - We can't sort all 65536 in one go. - But we can sort chunks of 1024 in shared memory, then merge chunks. This is a standard merge sort: 1. Sort chunks of 1024 in shared memory 2. Merge sorted chunks pairwise, then quadruple, etc. For M=65536, chunk=1024: - Level 0: 64 sorted chunks - Level 1: 32 chunks of 2048 - Level 2: 16 chunks of 4096 - Level 3: 8 chunks of 8192 - Level 4: 4 chunks of 16384 - Level 5: 2 chunks of 32768 - Level 6: 1 chunk of 65536 But we only need the top-64! After each merge, we can truncate to top-64. For merging two sorted lists of size 1024 into top-64: - Use a parallel merge: each thread finds its position in the merged list - Or use a bitonic merge network Actually, merging two sorted lists A and B into top-k is simple: - Compare A[0] with B[0], take larger. Then compare A[1] with B[0] (or A[0] with B[1]), etc. - This is a sequential merge, O(k) time. - With 64 threads, each thread can find one element of the top-64 in O(1) time if we use binary search. Wait, thread i can find the i-th largest element by finding the split point between A and B. For descending lists, the i-th largest is either A[a] or B[b] where a + b = i. We can binary search for the split. But this is complex to implement efficiently. Let me step back. What do the fastest GPU top-k implementations do? I know that NVIDIA's CUB library has a `DeviceRadixSort` and `DeviceSelect`. PyTorch's topk uses a radix select for large k and a bitonic sort for small k. For our case (small k: 1, 8, 16, 32, 64), the fastest approach on GPU is typically: 1. Each thread loads a chunk 2. Each thread maintains a small sorted array of top-k in registers 3. Threads merge within warps using shuffles or shared memory 4. Warps merge within blocks 5. Blocks write to global memory 6. A second kernel merges block results The key insight from CUB and other libraries is that they use **register-level sorting networks** for small arrays and **warp-level shuffles** for merging. Actually, I just realized something important. In my current implementation, the arrays are in local memory. But what if I use a **struct array** and access it with compile-time known indices? Wait, the compiler puts arrays in local memory when there's dynamic indexing. But in `heap_replace`, the index `pos` depends on the data. The compiler cannot know `pos` at compile time. However, for K=64, the heap has only 6 levels. I can manually unroll the entire heap operation without a loop: ```cuda // Level 0 int p0 = 0; int l0 = 1, r0 = 2; int s0 = p0; if (l0 < K && vals[l0] < v) s0 = l0; if (r0 < K && vals[r0] < v) s0 = r0; if (s0 == p0) { vals[p0] = v; idxs[p0] = idx; return; } vals[p0] = vals[s0]; idxs[p0] = idxs[s0]; // Level 1 int p1 = s0; int l1 = 2*p1+1, r1 = 2*p1+2; int s1 = p1; if (l1 < K && vals[l1] < v) s1 = l1; if (r1 < K && vals[r1] < v) s1 = r1; if (s1 == p1) { vals[p1] = v; idxs[p1] = idx; return; } vals[p1] = vals[s1]; idxs[p1] = idxs[s1]; // ... repeat for 6 levels ``` But `vals[l0]`, `vals[r0]`, etc. still use dynamic indices `l0`, `r0`, which depend on `s0`, which depends on data. The compiler still can't map these to specific registers. Unless K is very small (like 8), the compiler will still use local memory. Actually, wait. For K=8, ptxas shows 39 registers and 64 bytes stack frame. Even K=8 uses local memory! So manual unrolling won't help for K > 4 or so. The compiler simply refuses to map arrays to registers when there's any dynamic indexing, regardless of size. So for K >= 8, the arrays WILL be in local memory. Period. Given this constraint, how can I make local memory faster? 1. **Reduce the number of local memory accesses** 2. **Increase occupancy so local memory accesses are interleaved and L1 cache is well-utilized** For point 1: instead of a heap with up to 6 levels per element, use a simpler data structure. What if we just load all elements into local memory, sort them, and take top-k? For n/thread = 128 and K=64: load 128 elements, sort 128 elements using bitonic sort, take top-64. Sorting 128 elements with bitonic sort: 128 * 7 * 7 / 4 = 1568 comparators. vs. heap: 128 * 6 * 2 = 1536 comparisons (roughly). Comparable. But bitonic sort has NO dynamic indexing! All indices are compile-time known. Wait, but we need to load 128 elements into a local array first. That's 128 floats + 128 ints = 1024 bytes of local memory. Then bitonic sort on this local array... but the array is in local memory, so each comparator does 2 loads and 2 stores to local memory. Hmm, same problem. What if we load elements directly into registers using scalar variables? For chunk_size = 8: ```cuda float v0, v1, v2, v3, v4, v5, v6, v7; int i0, i1, i2, i3, i4, i5, i6, i7; // Load v0 = row_x[tid]; i0 = tid; v1 = row_x[tid+blockDim.x]; i1 = tid+blockDim.x; // ... // Bitonic sort of 8 // All indices are compile-time known, so all variables stay in registers! ``` Then after sorting 8 elements in registers, merge with top-64 (in local memory). The merge requires dynamic indexing. But we can process 8 elements at a time: load 8, sort 8, then merge the sorted 8 with the sorted 64. The merge can be done with a sequential loop over the 8 elements, inserting each into the 64-array. For each of the 8 elements: - Binary search its position in the 64-array: 6 comparisons - Shift elements to make room: up to 64 moves - Insert: 1 move - Total: ~71 operations per element, all on local memory. 8 elements * 71 = 568 operations. Plus bitonic sort of 8 = 36 comparators (in registers). Total ~604 operations per chunk. For 128 elements = 16 chunks: 16 * 604 = 9664 operations. vs. heap: 128 * 6 * 4 = 3072 operations. The heap is still fewer operations. But local memory operations are cached. Let me think about which is faster in practice. Actually, I realize the real issue might not be the heap algorithm itself, but the fact that with 128 threads and batch=1, we have terrible GPU utilization. Let me verify by checking what happens with batch=64, n=8192, k=8: - 64 blocks of 256 threads = 16384 threads total. - My kernel takes 0.059ms vs eager 0.049ms. Only 20% slower! - For this shape, local memory might still be a factor, but with many blocks, the GPU is well-utilized and the L1 cache is effective. For batch=1, n=131072, k=64: - 1 block of 128 threads = 128 threads total. - My kernel takes 1.709ms vs eager 0.055ms. 31x slower! - With only 1 block, the GPU is mostly idle. The single block has 4 warps, which can run on one SM. But the local memory accesses within those warps might be the bottleneck. So the primary issue for batch=1 is lack of parallelism, and the secondary issue is local memory latency. To fix batch=1, I MUST use multiple blocks per row. Let me design a multi-block approach. **Multi-block design for pass 1:** Launch `num_blocks` blocks per row. Each block has `THREADS` threads. - Block `b` processes elements `[b * chunk_size, (b+1) * chunk_size)`. - chunk_size = n / num_blocks. - Each thread within the block processes `chunk_size / THREADS` elements. - Each thread keeps top-k in local memory. - Block writes `THREADS * k` candidates to global memory. Actually, instead of having each thread write its top-k, we can do a block-level merge within each block to reduce candidates. This reduces pass 2 work. For block-level merge with `THREADS` threads, we merge `THREADS * k` candidates into `k` candidates using shared memory bitonic sort. This is what I already do in my single-block kernel. The shared memory for this is `THREADS * k * 8` bytes. For THREADS=256, k=64: 256 * 64 * 8 = 131072 bytes = 128KB. Too much for default, too much for opt-in (101KB). For THREADS=128, k=64: 128 * 64 * 8 = 65536 bytes = 64KB. Needs opt-in. For THREADS=64, k=64: 64 * 64 * 8 = 32768 bytes = 32KB. Fits in default! But with 64 threads per block, each thread processes more elements. For n=131072 and num_blocks=8: - chunk_size = 131072 / 8 = 16384 - Each thread processes 16384 / 64 = 256 elements - Heap work: 256 * 6 * 4 = ~6000 operations And we have 8 blocks! So 8 * 64 = 512 threads total. Much better parallelism. For pass 2: 8 blocks * 64 threads * 64 k = 32768 candidates per row. Pass 2 needs to find top-64 from 32768 candidates. 32768 candidates = 32768 * 8 = 256KB. Too much for shared memory. So pass 2 needs to stream through candidates in tiles. Pass 2 design: - Launch 1 block per row with 256 threads. - Shared memory: block top-k heap (64 * 8 = 512 bytes) + tile buffer. - Tile size: 1024 candidates = 8KB. - Stream through all 32768 candidates in tiles of 1024. - For each tile, each thread processes 4 candidates, checks if > heap root. - But the heap is shared! We need synchronization. Better pass 2 design: - Each thread maintains a local top-k from its portion of candidates. - Then block-level merge to final top-k. With 256 threads and 32768 candidates, each thread processes 128 candidates. - Each thread loads 128 candidates, keeps top-64 in local memory. - Block-level merge: 256 * 64 = 16384 candidates in shared memory. 128KB. Too much. Alternative: two-level merge in pass 2. - Warp-level merge first: each warp merges 32 * 64 = 2048 candidates into 64. - 8 warps produce 512 candidates. - Block-level merge: 512 candidates into 64. - 512 * 8 = 4KB shared memory. Easy! So pass 2 can be: 1. Each thread loads 128 candidates and keeps top-64 in local memory. 2. Warp-level bitonic sort of 32*64=2048 candidates in shared memory. 3. Each warp writes top-64 to block shared memory. 4. Block-level bitonic sort of 8*64=512 candidates. 5. Write top-64 to output. This is exactly the same as my original single-block design! The only difference is that pass 2 processes candidates instead of raw elements. And pass 2 has 256 threads (8 warps). Shared memory for warp sort: 8 * 2048 * 8 = 131072 bytes = 128KB. Too much! Wait, same shared memory issue. For 8 warps with warp_cand=2048 each, we need 8 * 2048 * 8 = 128KB for the warp work areas. This exceeds 101KB opt-in limit. Can we reduce the warp work area? Yes! Instead of having each warp sort 2048 candidates in its own shared memory area, we can sort them sequentially: - Warp 0 sorts its 2048 candidates in a shared memory area. - After warp 0 finishes, warp 1 sorts its 2048 candidates in the SAME area. - And so on. But warps in a block execute concurrently, not sequentially. We can't have them reuse the same area unless we synchronize between warps. Actually, we CAN synchronize warps using `__syncthreads()` between warp sorts. But this serializes the warps, reducing parallelism. For 8 warps serialized: 8x slowdown of the warp sort. But the warp sort is fast (~1-2 microseconds). 8x = ~16 microseconds. Still fast compared to the element processing. Wait, but with 256 threads, do we really need 8 separate warp sorts? What if we just use 1 warp to do all the merging? If we have 256 threads each with top-64 in local memory, we need to merge 256 * 64 = 16384 candidates. We can't sort all 16384 in shared memory at once (needs 128KB). But we can do it in stages: 1. Sort 1024 candidates at a time in shared memory, keep top-64. 2. Repeat 16 times. For each batch of 1024 candidates: - Load 1024 candidates into shared memory (8KB). - Bitonic sort 1024 in shared memory with 256 threads. - Take top-64. - Merge with current top-64. How to merge current top-64 with new top-64? - Both are sorted descending. - Sequential merge: 64 + 64 = 128 comparisons, produce new top-64. - But the current top-64 is in local memory (or we can keep it in registers if we use scalar variables... no, K=64 is too big for registers). Actually, we can keep the running top-64 in shared memory too! Shared memory for running top-64: 64 * 8 = 512 bytes. So pass 2 algorithm: 1. Allocate shared memory: s_cand_vals[1024], s_cand_idxs[1024], s_top_vals[64], s_top_idxs[64]. 2. Initialize s_top to -inf. 3. For each batch of 1024 candidates (from global memory): a. Load 1024 candidates into s_cand. b. Bitonic sort s_cand descending. c. Merge s_top (64) with s_cand top-64 (64) into new s_top. 4. Write s_top to output. Total shared memory: 1024*4 + 1024*4 + 64*4 + 64*4 = 8192 + 256 + 256 = 8704 bytes = 8.5KB. Very small! For M=32768 candidates, we have 32 batches of 1024. Each batch: - Sort 1024: 1024 * 10 * 10 / 4 = 25600 comparisons. With 256 threads, ~100 operations per thread. - Merge 64+64: 128 comparisons. With 256 threads, we can parallelize but it's small. - Total per batch: ~100 operations per thread. - 32 batches: ~3200 operations per thread. - At 1.5GHz: ~2 microseconds. This is fast! And it uses very little shared memory. Now, for the merge of two sorted 64-element lists into one 64-element list, we can do: - Thread i (0 <= i < 64) finds the i-th largest element. - To find the i-th largest in the union of A[0..63] and B[0..63]: - We can binary search in A: for A[j], there are j elements in A larger than A[j], and up to 64 elements in B larger than A[j]. - The rank of A[j] in the union is j + (# of B elements > A[j]). - We want elements with rank < 64. Actually, a simpler parallel merge: - For each element A[j], count how many B elements are >= A[j]. - The position of A[j] in the merged array is j + count. But this requires binary search in B for each A[j]. For 64 elements and 64 threads, each thread does one binary search (6 comparisons). Total: 64 * 6 = 384 comparisons. Very fast! But implementing this in shared memory with synchronization is a bit complex. Let me use a simpler approach: just do a sequential merge with one thread, since it's only 128 comparisons. ```cuda if (tid == 0) { float a[64], b[64]; // copy s_top and s_cand_top to a and b int ai = 0, bi = 0; for (int i = 0; i < 64; i++) { if (ai < 64 && (bi >= 64 || a[ai] >= b[bi])) { s_top_vals[i] = a[ai]; s_top_idxs[i] = a_idx[ai]; ai++; } else { s_top_vals[i] = b[bi]; s_top_idxs[i] = b_idx[bi]; bi++; } } } __syncthreads(); ``` This is 128 operations for one thread. At 1.5GHz, 0.1 microseconds. Negligible. So pass 2 is dominated by sorting 1024 elements, 32 times. 32 * 25600 comparisons = ~819200 comparisons total. With 256 threads: 3200 comparisons per thread. At 1 comparison per clock: 2 microseconds. Fast! Now let me design the full two-pass kernel. **Pass 1: Multi-block local top-k** - Launch `batch * num_blocks` blocks. - Each block has `THREADS` threads (e.g., 128 or 256). - Block `b` of row `r` processes elements `[b * n/num_blocks, (b+1) * n/num_blocks)`. - Each thread keeps top-k in local memory (heap). - After processing, each thread's top-k is written to global memory at: `tmp_vals[((r * num_blocks + b) * THREADS + tid) * k + i]` No block-level merge in pass 1. Just thread-level top-k written directly. **Pass 2: Global merge** - Launch `batch` blocks. - Each block has `THREADS` threads (e.g., 256). - Shared memory: s_cand[1024] vals + s_cand[1024] idxs + s_top[64] vals + s_top[64] idxs = ~8704 bytes. - Initialize s_top to -inf. - Stream through `num_blocks * THREADS * k` candidates in tiles of 1024. - For each tile: 1. Load into s_cand 2. Bitonic sort s_cand descending 3. Sequential merge s_top with s_cand[0..63] - Write s_top to output. Wait, but we have `num_blocks * THREADS * k` candidates. For batch=1, n=131072, k=64, num_blocks=8, THREADS=128: - Candidates = 8 * 128 * 64 = 65536. - Tiles of 1024: 64 tiles. - Pass 2 time: 64 * sort_1024 + 64 * merge_64. - sort_1024 = 25600 comparators. With 256 threads, ~100 comparators per thread. - 64 * 100 = 6400 comparators per thread. - At 1.5GHz: ~4 microseconds. Fast! And pass 1: - 8 blocks, 128 threads each. - Each thread processes 131072 / (8 * 128) = 128 elements. - Heap: 128 * 6 * 4 = ~3000 operations per thread. - With 8 blocks running on 8 SMs (or more if occupancy allows), total time ~2 microseconds. Total: ~6 microseconds. Compared to torch.topk's ~55 microseconds. We'd be 9x faster! Wait, that seems too optimistic. Let me recalculate. For pass 1 with local memory heap: - Each heap_replace does ~6 iterations. - Each iteration accesses local memory: read vals[left], vals[right], write vals[pos], read idxs[left]... - Local memory is cached in L1. Each access is ~10-20 cycles. - 128 elements * 6 iterations * 4 accesses * 15 cycles = ~46080 cycles per thread. - At 1.5GHz: ~30 microseconds per thread. - With 8 blocks on 8 SMs: ~30 microseconds total. For pass 2: - 64 tiles * sort_1024. - Each sort_1024 comparison does 2 shared memory reads, 2 shared memory writes. - Shared memory is ~20 cycles. - 64 tiles * 25600 comps * 4 accesses * 20 cycles = ~131M cycles... wait, that's total across all threads. - With 256 threads: 131M / 256 = 512K cycles per thread. - At 1.5GHz: ~340 microseconds per thread. - But only 1 block! So 340 microseconds. Hmm, 340 microseconds for pass 2 is not great. But it's still better than 1700 microseconds. Actually, let me recalculate more carefully. A bitonic sort of 1024 with 256 threads: - Number of stages: log2(1024) * (log2(1024)+1) / 2 = 10 * 11 / 2 = 55 stages. - Each stage: 1024 / 2 = 512 comparators. - With 256 threads, each thread does 512 / 256 = 2 comparators per stage. - Total: 55 * 2 = 110 comparators per thread. - Each comparator: 2 reads, 1 comparison, 2 writes (if swap). Let's say 5 operations. - 110 * 5 = 550 operations per thread. - 64 tiles: 64 * 550 = 35200 operations per thread. - At 1.5GHz: ~23 microseconds. That's much more reasonable! And with shared memory, each operation is fast. So pass 2: ~23 microseconds. Pass 1: ~30 microseconds. Total: ~53 microseconds. Compare to torch.topk: ~55 microseconds. So we'd be roughly equal, not 9x faster. But wait, torch.topk is only getting 10 GB/s. If we can get closer to peak bandwidth (1800 GB/s), we'd be much faster. For 512KB input, at 1800 GB/s = 0.28 microseconds. But the comparison and selection overhead makes this impossible. Actually, for pass 1, if we use many more blocks, we can get better memory bandwidth utilization. With num_blocks=32 instead of 8: - 32 blocks, 128 threads each. - Each thread processes 32 elements. - Heap: 32 * 6 * 4 * 15 cycles = ~11520 cycles = 7.7 microseconds. - 32 blocks running in parallel on 32 SMs: ~7.7 microseconds. - Candidates = 32 * 128 * 64 = 262144. - Pass 2 tiles = 262144 / 1024 = 256 tiles. - Pass 2 time = 256 * 550 ops * 5 cycles / 256 threads = ~2750 cycles per thread = 1.8 microseconds... wait no. - 256 tiles * 110 comparators per thread = 28160 comparators per thread. - 28160 * 5 cycles = 140800 cycles = 94 microseconds. Hmm, with more blocks in pass 1, pass 1 gets faster but pass 2 gets slower. There's a tradeoff. For num_blocks=8: total ~53 microseconds. For num_blocks=16: pass 1 ~15 us, pass 2 ~47 us, total ~62 us. For num_blocks=32: total ~102 us. So num_blocks=8 seems optimal for this shape. But ~53 microseconds is still roughly equal to torch.topk. We want to beat it. How can we make pass 1 faster? The bottleneck is local memory accesses in the heap. What if we use a different algorithm for pass 1 that doesn't need a heap? For small chunk_size (like 32 elements), each thread can just load all 32 elements, sort them, and keep top-64 (which is just the 32 elements + padding). No heap needed! For chunk_size = 32 and K = 64: 1. Load 32 elements into `chunk_vals[32]`, `chunk_idxs[32]` (local memory). 2. Fill remaining 32 with -inf. 3. Bitonic sort 64 in local memory. Wait, but `chunk_vals[32]` and the sort are still in local memory. Dynamic indexing in bitonic sort! The compiler will put `chunk_vals` in local memory. Same problem. But what if chunk_size is small enough that the compiler maps it to registers? For 32 floats + 32 ints = 256 bytes. The compiler might still put it in local memory if there's dynamic indexing. Actually, ptxas showed that even K=8 (64 bytes) uses local memory. So any array with dynamic indexing goes to local memory, regardless of size. Given this constraint, the only way to avoid local memory is to avoid dynamic indexing entirely. This means: 1. All array indices must be compile-time constants. 2. No loops with dynamic iteration counts that access arrays. For top-k with K=64, this means we cannot use arrays for the top-k storage. We must use scalar variables. But with 64 scalar variables, writing a generic kernel is extremely tedious. However, we can use macros or template metaprogramming to generate the code. Wait, there's another option: use Triton. Triton handles registers automatically and can do block-level operations efficiently. But implementing top-k in Triton is also complex. Actually, I just had another idea. What if I use **warp-level parallel sorting**? With 32 threads in a warp, we can sort up to 32 elements entirely in registers using warp shuffles. Each thread holds one element. The bitonic sort network uses warp shuffles to exchange elements between threads. For sorting 32 elements in a warp: - Bitonic sort of 32 requires log2(32) * (log2(32)+1) / 2 = 15 stages. - Each stage: some threads exchange elements with other threads using `__shfl_sync`. - No shared memory or local memory needed for the elements themselves! But we also need indices. Each thread can hold one (value, index) pair. Exchanges use `__shfl_sync` for both value and index. This is brilliant! For K=32, we can sort exactly one warp's worth of elements entirely in registers using shuffles. For K=64, we'd need 2 warps. But we need to MERGE the sorted warp results with the running top-k. And the running top-k needs to be stored somewhere. What if we maintain the running top-k across multiple warps in shared memory, but the per-chunk sorting is done in registers with shuffles? Actually, let me think about a different architecture entirely. **Warp-level streaming top-k with shuffle-based merge** Each warp (32 threads) processes a stream of elements: 1. Each thread loads one element. 2. The warp sorts the 32 elements using shuffle-based bitonic sort. 3. The sorted 32 elements are merged with the warp's running top-k in shared memory. 4. Repeat. For K=64, the running top-k is 64 elements. We can store it in shared memory: 64 * 8 = 512 bytes per warp. For 8 warps: 4KB. Very small! The merge of sorted 32 with sorted 64 into sorted 64: - We can use a bitonic merge of 96 elements (or just sequential merge). - With 32 threads, each thread can participate in the merge. But the merge still needs dynamic indexing or complex shuffle patterns. Actually, for merging sorted A (size 32) and sorted B (size 64) into top-64, we can: 1. Concatenate A and B. 2. Use a partial bitonic sort that only keeps the top 64. But implementing partial bitonic sort with shuffles is complex. Let me look at this from yet another angle. What if I use **CUDA cooperative groups** or just simple warp shuffles to do a full sort of all candidates? For a block with 256 threads and K=64: - Total candidates after all threads process their chunks: 256 * 64 = 16384. - We can't sort 16384 with 256 threads in shared memory (needs 128KB). But what if we use **multiple warps to sort in a tree fashion**? - Each warp sorts its 64 candidates using a shuffle-based sort. - Then warps merge pairwise. For warp sort of 64 with 32 threads: - Each thread holds 2 elements (since 64 / 32 = 2). - Bitonic sort of 64 with each thread holding 2 elements. - Compare-and-swap within a thread (no shuffle) or across threads (shuffle). This is possible! Each thread stores 2 values in registers. For a compare-swap between thread i and thread j, we shuffle the values. For bitonic sort of 64 with 32 threads (2 elements per thread): - There are log2(64) stages. - Some comparators are within a thread (between its 2 elements). - Some comparators are between threads (using shuffles). - The pattern is known and can be hardcoded. After the warp sort, each warp has 64 sorted elements. Then we need to merge 8 warps' results (512 elements) into top-64. We can do this with shared memory: load all 512 into shared memory, bitonic sort 512, take top-64. Shared memory for 512 elements: 512 * 8 = 4KB. Very small! And for the per-thread element processing, each thread still needs a local top-k of 64. But instead of a heap, each thread can: 1. Load elements into a local buffer. 2. Sort the buffer using a local bitonic sort. 3. The buffer is in local memory, so it's slow. Wait, but if each thread only processes a small number of elements (like 32), it can load them into scalar registers and sort them without arrays! For chunk_size = 16: ```cuda float v0 = row_x[base + 0]; float v1 = row_x[base + 1]; // ... v15 int i0 = base + 0; // ... i15 // Bitonic sort of 16 using scalar variables // All operations are on scalars in registers! CMP_SWAP(v0, v1); CMP_SWAP(v2, v3); ... // stage 1 CMP_SWAP(v0, v2); CMP_SWAP(v1, v3); ... // stage 2 // etc. ``` Then merge the sorted 16 with the running top-64. But the running top-64 is in local memory. Hmm. What if each thread processes 16 elements, sorts them in registers, then writes them to shared memory. Then the block merges all threads' sorted chunks? For 256 threads * 16 elements = 4096 elements per block. Block-level merge of 4096 sorted 16-element lists into top-64. This is complex but possible. We can do it in stages: 1. Each warp merges 32 sorted 16-element lists into one sorted 512-element list? No, we only need top-64. 2. A warp can merge 32 sorted 16-element lists into top-64 by loading all 512 candidates and doing a selection. With 32 threads in a warp, each loads 16 candidates = 512 total. Warp-level selection of top-64 from 512: - We can use a parallel tournament tree or repeated max reduction. - 64 iterations of finding the max among 512 elements. - Each iteration: tree reduction over 512 elements with 32 threads. - 512 / 32 = 16 local comparisons per thread. - Warp shuffle reduction: 5 stages. - Total per iteration: ~21 operations. - 64 iterations: ~1344 operations per thread. - Plus setting the found max to -inf. With 8 warps doing this in parallel: each warp processes 512 candidates and produces top-64. Then we have 8 * 64 = 512 candidates. Block-level merge of 512 candidates into top-64 using shared memory bitonic sort: 4KB shared memory, fast. Total operations per block: - Element loading: 4096 loads - Warp-level selection: 8 warps * 1344 ops = ~10752 ops - Block-level sort: 512-element bitonic sort = ~2304 ops - Total: ~13000 ops for the block - With 256 threads: ~50 ops per thread. Let me formalize it. **New kernel design: "Warp-Select Top-K"** For each block: 1. Divide block into warps (e.g., 8 warps of 32 threads for 256 threads). 2. Each thread loads `ELEMENTS_PER_THREAD` (e.g., 16) elements into scalar registers. 3. Sort the thread's elements using scalar bitonic sort (all in registers). 4. Write sorted elements to warp shared memory. 5. Each warp performs warp-level selection of top-k from all threads in the warp: - Load all candidates into registers/shuffles. - Repeatedly find max using warp shuffle reduction, k times. - After finding each max, mark it as -inf. 6. Write warp top-k to block shared memory. 7. Block-level bitonic sort of all warp top-k arrays to produce final top-k. Wait, steps 3-5 can be optimized. This is very promising. But I need to implement the warp-level selection carefully. **Warp-level selection algorithm:** Input: `warp_vals[M]` and `warp_idxs[M]` in shared memory (M = 32 * EPT). Output: top-k values and indices, stored in shared memory or registers. Method: k iterations of parallel max-finding with masking. ```cuda // Each thread maintains local max float local_max = -FLT_MAX; int local_idx = -1; // k iterations for (int iter = 0; iter < k; iter++) { // Each thread finds max of its portion local_max = -FLT_MAX; for (int i = lane; i < M; i += 32) { if (s_vals[i] > local_max) { local_max = s_vals[i]; local_idx = s_vals[i]; // actually index } } // Warp reduction to find global max #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { float other_max = __shfl_down_sync(mask, local_max, offset); int other_idx = __shfl_down_sync(mask, local_idx, offset); if (other_max > local_max) { local_max = other_max; local_idx = other_idx; } } // Broadcast global max and its index to all threads float global_max = __shfl_sync(mask, local_max, 0); int global_idx = __shfl_sync(mask, local_idx, 0); // Record in output if (lane == 0) { out_vals[iter] = global_max; out_idxs[iter] = global_idx; } // Mark the found element as -inf // We need to know which thread owns it int owner = __shfl_sync(mask, 0, 0); // Wait, we need the lane that found it // Actually, after reduction, lane 0 has the global max. // But we need to know which element in shared memory it was. // We can have lane 0 also track the shared memory index. } ``` Wait, there's a subtle issue. After warp reduction, lane 0 has the global max and its `local_idx`. But `local_idx` is the index within the thread's portion, not the shared memory index. We need the shared memory index to set it to -inf. We can track the shared memory index instead: ```cuda int local_sm_idx = -1; for (int i = lane; i < M; i += 32) { if (s_vals[i] > local_max) { local_max = s_vals[i]; local_sm_idx = i; } } ``` Then after warp reduction, lane 0 has `local_sm_idx` which is the shared memory index. We broadcast this to all threads, and the thread with `lane == (local_sm_idx % 32)` sets `s_vals[local_sm_idx] = -FLT_MAX`. Wait, but `local_sm_idx` might not be in lane 0's portion. After reduction, lane 0 has the winning `local_sm_idx`. We can broadcast it: ```cuda int winner_sm_idx = __shfl_sync(mask, local_sm_idx, 0); if (lane == (winner_sm_idx & 31)) { s_vals[winner_sm_idx] = -FLT_MAX; } ``` This works! Only one thread writes to shared memory. However, there's a problem: what if two elements have the same max value? The reduction picks one arbitrarily, which is fine. The key components are: 1. **Pass 1 kernel**: processes a chunk of elements, produces top-k. - Inputs: `x`, `row`, `start`, `end`, `k` - Outputs: `tmp_vals[(row * num_blocks + block) * k + i]`, `tmp_idxs[...]` 2. **Pass 2 kernel**: merges candidates into final top-k. - Inputs: `tmp_vals`, `tmp_idxs`, `num_candidates`, `k` - Outputs: `out_vals`, `out_idxs` For pass 1, the warp-select algorithm: ```cuda __global__ void topk_pass1(const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ tmp_vals, int* __restrict__ tmp_idxs, int n, int k, int num_blocks_per_row) { int row = blockIdx.x / num_blocks_per_row; int block_in_row = blockIdx.x % num_blocks_per_row; int block_start = block_in_row * n / num_blocks_per_row; int block_end = (block_in_row + 1) * n / num_blocks_per_row; int block_n = block_end - block_start; const float* row_x = x + row * n + block_start; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; int num_warps = blockDim.x >> 5; // Shared memory extern __shared__ char smem[]; // Warp areas int EPT = (block_n + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; // elements per thread, rounded up // But EPT might vary. Now let me think about the pass 1 kernel more carefully. **Pass 1 kernel for k > 1:** ```cuda template <int K> __global__ void topk_pass1(const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ tmp_vals, int* __restrict__ tmp_idxs, int n, int num_blocks_per_row) { int row = blockIdx.x / num_blocks_per_row; i < num_warps * M; i += blockDim.x) { s_warp_vals[i] = -FLT_MAX; } __syncthreads(); // Load elements into warp shared memory int warp_start = warp_id * EPT; for (int j = 0; j < EPT; j++) { int idx = warp_start + j * 32 + lane; iter < K; iter++) { local_max = -FLT_MAX; local_sm_idx = -1; for (int i = lane; i < M; i += 32) { if (my_warp_vals[i] > local_max) { local_max = my_warp_vals[i]; local_sm_idx = i; } } // Warp reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; s_block_vals[i] = s_block_vals[ixj]; s_block_vals[ixj] = tv; int ti = s_block_idxs[i]; s_block_idxs[i] = s_block_idxs[ixj]; s_block_idxs[ixj] = ti; } } } } __syncthreads(); } } // Write top-K to global memory if (tid < K) { int out_pos = (row * num_blocks_per_row + block_in_row) * K + tid; tmp_vals[out_pos] = s_block_vals[tid]; tmp_idxs[out_pos] = s_block_idxs[tid]; } } ``` Wait, there's an issue. In the warp-level selection, after finding the winner, I write to `s_block_vals[warp_id * K + iter]`. But `iter` goes from 0 to K-1, and `warp_id` is 0-3. So `s_block_vals` needs to be `num_warps * K` elements. Yes. But the bitonic sort operates on `s_block_vals[0..block_cand-1]` where `block_cand = num_warps * K`. After the sort, `s_block_vals[0..K-1]` contains the top-K of the block. We write these to global memory. For k=64 and num_warps=4, block_cand=256. The sort produces top-256, but we only need top-64. But wait, we need `s_block_vals` to be sorted descending for the top-K to be at the beginning. The bitonic sort does sort descending. Good. One issue: in the warp-level selection, we write candidates to `s_block_vals` in the order we find them (largest first). So they're already sorted descending within each warp's segment. Now for pass 2: ```cuda template <int K, int TILE> __global__ void topk_pass2(const float* __restrict__ tmp_vals, const int* __restrict__ tmp_idxs, float* __restrict__ out_vals, int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs, int num_candidates_per_row) { int row = blockIdx.x; Each warp should load a DIFFERENT portion of the block. Warp 0 should load `row_x[0..191]`, warp 1 should load `row_x[192..383]`, etc. I need to add a warp offset to the load: ```cuda int warp_start = warp_id * (block_n / num_warps); // Or more precisely: int warp_chunk = (block_n + num_warps - 1) / num_warps; int warp_start = warp_id * warp_chunk; int warp_end = min(warp_start + warp_chunk, block_n); ``` But wait, my current code has: ```cuda int warp_off = warp_id * M; for (int j = 0; j < EPT; ++j) { int idx = j * 32 + lane; ... s_warp_vals[warp_off + j * 32 + lane] = val; s_warp_idxs[warp_off + j * 32 + lane] = idx_out; } ``` `warp_off` is used for the SHARED MEMORY offset, not for the GLOBAL MEMORY offset! The global memory index is just `idx`, not `warp_start + idx`. So all warps read the same global memory elements but write to different shared memory regions. This means each warp is processing the SAME elements, not different ones. For example, with block_n=512 and 4 warps: - Warp 0 loads elements 0..127 into shared memory 0..127. - Warp 1 loads elements 0..127 into shared memory 128..255. - Warp 2 loads elements 0..127 into shared memory 256..383. - Warp 3 loads elements 0..127 into shared memory 384..511. Then each warp selects top-64 from its 128 elements. But all warps are selecting from the SAME 128 elements! So the block has 4 copies of the top-64 of the first 128 elements, not the top-64 of all 512 elements. This explains the duplicates! And it explains why the output values are wrong (only using a subset of elements). The fix is to have each warp load a different portion of the block: ```cuda int warp_chunk = (block_n + num_warps - 1) / num_warps; int warp_start = warp_id * warp_chunk; int warp_end = min(warp_start + warp_chunk, block_n); int warp_n = warp_end - warp_start; for (int j = 0; j < EPT; ++j) { int idx = warp_start + j * 32 + lane; float val = -FLT_MAX; int idx_out = -1; if (idx < warp_end) { val = row_x[idx]; idx_out = block_start + idx; } s_warp_vals[warp_off + j * 32 + lane] = val; s_warp_idxs[warp_off + j * 32 + lane] = idx_out; } ``` But wait, `EPT` is computed based on `block_n`, not `warp_n`. But this makes shared memory layout per-warp non-uniform. It's easier to use a uniform `M` for all warps. So I'll keep `M = 32 * EPT` where `EPT = (block_n + 127) / 128`, but add `warp_start` to the global memory load. Actually, there's a subtlety. `block_n` is the number of elements in the block. `block_n / num_warps` might not be divisible by 32. So `warp_start` might not be aligned to 32. Coalesced! Perfect. But thread 0 in the second iteration reads row_x[188 + 32] = row_x[220]. Also consecutive. Coalesced. So the load is coalesced regardless of warp_start alignment. Great. For pass 2 kernel: ```cuda template <int K, int TILE> __global__ void topk_pass2(const float* __restrict__ tmp_vals, const int* __restrict__ tmp_idxs, float* __restrict__ out_vals, int64_t* __restrict__ out_idxs, int num_candidates_per_row) { int row = blockIdx.x; const float* row_vals = tmp_vals + row * num_candidates_per_row; const int* row_idxs = tmp_idxs + row * num_candidates_per_row; int tid = threadIdx.x; extern __shared__ char smem[]; float* s_tile_vals = reinterpret_cast<float*>(smem); int* s_tile_idxs = reinterpret_cast<int*>(smem + TILE * sizeof(float)); float* s_run_vals = reinterpret_cast<float*>(smem + TILE * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int))); int* s_run_idxs = reinterpret_cast<int*>(s_run_vals + K); for (int i = tid; i < K; i += blockDim.x) { s_run_vals[i] = -FLT_MAX; s_run_idxs[i] = -1; } __syncthreads(); int num_tiles = (num_candidates_per_row + TILE - 1) / TILE; for (int t = 0; t < num_tiles; t++) { int tile_start = t * TILE; int tile_n = min(TILE, num_candidates_per_row - tile_start); // Load tile for (int i = tid; i < TILE; i += blockDim.x) { if (i < tile_n) { s_tile_vals[i] = row_vals[tile_start + i]; s_tile_idxs[i] = row_idxs[tile_start + i]; } else { s_tile_vals[i] = -FLT_MAX; s_tile_idxs[i] = -1; } } __syncthreads(); // Bitonic sort tile descending for (int kk = 2; kk <= TILE; kk <<= 1) { for (int j = kk >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) { for (int i = tid; i < TILE; i += blockDim.x) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { if ((i & kk) == 0) { if (s_tile_vals[i] < s_tile_vals[ixj]) { float tv = s_tile_vals[i]; s_tile_vals[i] = s_tile_vals[ixj]; s_tile_vals[ixj] = tv; int ti = s_tile_idxs[i]; s_tile_idxs[i] = s_tile_idxs[ixj]; s_tile_idxs[ixj] = ti; } } else { if (s_tile_vals[i] > s_tile_vals[ixj]) { float tv = s_tile_vals[i]; s_tile_vals[i] = s_tile_vals[ixj]; s_tile_vals[ixj] = tv; int ti = s_tile_idxs[i]; s_tile_idxs[i] = s_tile_idxs[ixj]; s_tile_idxs[ixj] = ti; } } } } __syncthreads(); } } // Merge s_tile[0..K-1] with s_run[0..K-1] if (tid == 0) { float a_vals[64]; int a_idxs[64]; for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { a_vals[i] = s_run_vals[i]; a_idxs[i] = s_run_idxs[i]; } int a = 0, b = 0; for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { bool take_a = (a < K) && (b >= K || a_vals[a] >= s_tile_vals[b]); if (take_a) { s_run_vals[i] = a_vals[a]; s_run_idxs[i] = a_idxs[a]; a++; } else { s_run_vals[i] = s_tile_vals[b]; s_run_idxs[i] = s_tile_idxs[b]; b++; } } } __syncthreads(); } if (tid < K) { out_vals[row * K + tid] = s_run_vals[tid]; out_idxs[row * K + tid] = static_cast<int64_t>(s_run_idxs[tid]); } } ``` Wait, but the bitonic sort of 4096 has `kk` going up to 4096. For j=2048, `i ^ j` might be large. With `TILE=4096` and `blockDim.x=256`, each thread processes 16 elements per stage. For j=2048, each thread compares `i` and `i^2048`. If both are in the tile, it swaps if needed. But `i` and `i^2048` are 2048 apart. Bank conflict: both map to the same bank if 2048 % 32 == 0. Yes! 2048 is a multiple of 32. So j=2048 causes 2-way intra-thread bank conflict. But as I analyzed before, this is only a 2x slowdown for a few stages. Actually, for TILE=4096, the number of stages with j >= 32: - kk=64: j=32. 1 stage. - kk=128: j=64, 32. 2 stages. - kk=256: j=128, 64, 32. 3 stages. - kk=512: j=256, 128, 64, 32. 4 stages. - kk=1024: j=512, 256, 128, 64, 32. 5 stages. - kk=2048: j=1024, 512, 256, 128, 64, 32. 6 stages. - kk=4096: j=2048, 1024, 512, 256, 128, 64, 32. 7 stages. Total conflicting stages: 1+2+3+4+5+6+7 = 28 stages. Total stages: 6+7+8+9+10+11+12 = 63 stages. Fraction: 28/63 = 44%. Slowdown: 0.44 * 2 + 0.56 * 1 = 1.44x. So bitonic sort of 4096 is ~1.44x slower than ideal. Still manageable. For 4 tiles: 4 * 63 stages * 4096/2 / 256 threads = 4 * 63 * 8 = 2016 comparators per thread. At 5 ops: ~10000 ops per thread. At 1.5GHz: ~6.7 microseconds. Plus 4 merges: negligible. Total: ~7 microseconds. This is much better than 121 microseconds! But I need to be careful. import torch
import solution

shapes = [
    (1, 131072, 64),
    (64, 8192, 8),
    (32, 16384, 32),
    (16, 12000, 16),
    (128, 4096, 1),
]

for batch, n, k in shapes:
    x = torch.randn(batch, n, device='cuda')
    model = solution.Model(batch, n, k).cuda()
    
    # Warmup
    for _ in range(10):
        model(x)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    with torch.profiler.profile(
        activities=[torch.profiler.ProfilerActivity.CUDA],
        record_shapes=False,
        with_stack=False
    ) as prof:
        for _ in range(10):
            model(x)
    
    events = prof.key_averages()
    cuda_time = sum(e.cuda_time_total for e in events) / 10.0
    print(f'({batch},{n},{k}): total_cuda={cuda_time:.1f}us')
    for e in events:
        if 'topk' in e.name:
            print(f'  {e.name.split(\"(\" )[0]}: {e.cuda_time_avg:.1f}us')

The bitonic sort is more regular and might compile to much more efficient code. Let me rewrite pass 2 to use bitonic sort of tiles (TILE=1024) and then merge top-K. This eliminates all the warp shuffles. Actually, I just realized another issue. In pass 2, the warp select does: ```cuda for (int iter = 0; iter < K; ++iter) { ... if (lane == (winner_pos & 31)) { my_tile_vals[winner_pos] = -FLT_MAX; } __syncwarp(); } ``` But `my_tile_vals` is a pointer into shared memory: `s_tile_vals + warp_id * warp_tile`. The pointer arithmetic `my_tile_vals[winner_pos]` computes the address dynamically. This might cause the compiler to keep the pointer in a register and do dynamic addressing. With 142 registers, the compiler is definitely doing something complex. But 0 spills means everything fits. Let me just try the bitonic sort approach for pass 2 with TILE=1024. I think it will be much faster. For TILE=1024: - Shared memory: 1024 * 8 + K * 8 = 8192 + 512 + 64*8 = 8192 + 512 + 512 = 9216 bytes for k=64. - Wait, I also need s_run_vals and s_run_idxs: K * 8 = 512 bytes. - Total: 1024 * 8 + 512 = 8704 bytes? No: - s_tile_vals: 1024 * 4 = 4096 - s_tile_idxs: 1024 * 4 = 4096 - s_run_vals: 64 * 4 = 256 - s_run_idxs: 64 * 4 = 256 - Total: 8704 bytes = 8.5KB. For num_candidates=16384: 16 tiles. Each tile: sort 1024 elements. 1024 * 10 * 10 / 4 = 25600 comparators. With 256 threads: 100 comparators per thread. 16 tiles: 1600 comparators per thread. Plus 16 merges: ~2000 comparisons (1 thread). Total: ~1600 * 5 = 8000 ops per thread. At 1.5GHz: ~5.3 microseconds. This should be much faster than 121 microseconds! Let me implement it. Wait, but for the merge, I need both s_run and s_tile_top to be accessible. After sorting the tile, s_tile_vals[0..K-1] contains the top-K. I can merge directly without copying. The merge can be done in-place or with a temporary array in shared memory. But since I need to preserve s_tile_vals for the next tile, I should merge into a temporary and then copy back. Actually, I can just do: ```cuda if (tid == 0) { float merged_vals[64]; int merged_idxs[64]; int a = 0, b = 0; for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { bool take_a = (a < K) && (b >= K || s_run_vals[a] >= s_tile_vals[b]); if (take_a) { merged_vals[i] = s_run_vals[a]; merged_idxs[i] = s_run_idxs[a]; a++; } else { merged_vals[i] = s_tile_vals[b]; merged_idxs[i] = s_tile_idxs[b]; b++; } } for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { s_run_vals[i] = merged_vals[i]; s_run_idxs[i] = merged_idxs[i]; } } ``` This uses a local array `merged_vals[64]` and `merged_idxs[64]`. With K=64, that's 128 values = 512 bytes. The compiler will put this in local memory (stack frame). But local memory for 512 bytes should be fast (L1 cached). Wait, actually for K=64, the arrays are `float merged_vals[64]` and `int merged_idxs[64]`. The indexing is dynamic (`merged_vals[a]` where `a` is computed at runtime). So the compiler will put these in local memory. But 512 bytes of local memory is tiny. It should be very fast. Alternatively, I can do the merge directly without a temporary: ```cuda if (tid == 0) { float run_vals_copy[64]; int run_idxs_copy[64]; for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { run_vals_copy[i] = s_run_vals[i]; run_idxs_copy[i] = s_run_idxs[i]; } int a = 0, b = 0; for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { bool take_a = (a < K) && (b >= K || run_vals_copy[a] >= s_tile_vals[b]); if (take_a) { s_run_vals[i] = run_vals_copy[a]; s_run_idxs[i] = run_idxs_copy[a]; a++; } else { s_run_vals[i] = s_tile_vals[b]; s_run_idxs[i] = s_tile_idxs[b]; b++; } } } ``` This copies s_run to local memory first, then merges into s_run. Still uses local memory but only for the copy. For small K (8, 16), the local memory is even smaller. OK, let me rewrite pass 2 with bitonic sort. reasoning (32869 chars)
The performance got WORSE! Shape 0 went from 0.152ms to 0.525ms. Shape 1 went from 0.022ms to 0.104ms. What happened? The bitonic sort approach is much slower than expected. Why? For shape 0, pass 2 now sorts 1024 elements 16 times. Each sort has 55 stages with `__syncthreads()` between each stage. That's 880 `__syncthreads()` calls in pass 2 alone! `__syncthreads()` is expensive! Each call might take 1-2 microseconds. 880 * 1us = 880us. That's close to the 525us we see. Ah! `__syncthreads()` is the killer. In the bitonic sort, we have log2(N) * (log2(N)+1) / 2 stages, each with a `__syncthreads()`. For TILE=1024, that's 55 `__syncthreads()` calls per tile. For 16 tiles: 880 `__syncthreads()` calls. Each `__syncthreads()` requires all threads in the block to reach the barrier. With 256 threads = 8 warps, if even one warp is delayed, all others wait. On modern GPUs, `__syncthreads()` is not free - it can take hundreds of cycles. For pass 1, M_PAD=128. Bitonic sort of 128 has 21 stages with `__syncwarp()`. `__syncwarp()` is cheaper than `__syncthreads()` because it only synchronizes one warp. 21 * 256 blocks = 5376 `__syncwarp()` calls. For pass 2 with 4 tiles and 8 warps: 4 * 64 * 8 = 2048 `__syncwarp()` calls. That's fewer than 880 `__syncthreads()` calls? Hmm, but `__syncthreads()` is much more expensive. Actually, wait. The previous version had 121us for pass 2. The new version has 525us. So the bitonic sort with `__syncthreads()` is 4x slower than warp select with `__syncwarp()`. This means `__syncthreads()` is indeed very expensive. To fix this, I need to reduce the number of synchronizations. Options: 1. Go back to warp select but optimize it. 2.
