The kernel for pass 2 reduction: ```cuda template <int K, int CHUNK> __global__ void topk_reduce(const float* __restrict__ in_vals, const int* __restrict__ in_idxs, float* __restrict__ out_vals, int* __restrict__ out_idxs, int num_candidates) { int row = blockIdx.y; int block_in_row = blockIdx.x; int chunk_start = block_in_row * CHUNK; int chunk_n = min(CHUNK, num_candidates - chunk_start); const float* row_vals = in_vals + row * num_candidates + chunk_start; const int* row_idxs = in_idxs + row * num_candidates + chunk_start; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; int num_warps = blockDim.x >> 5; extern __shared__ char smem[]; float* s_vals = reinterpret_cast<float*>(smem); int* s_idxs = reinterpret_cast<int*>(smem + CHUNK * sizeof(float)); float* s_out_vals = reinterpret_cast<float*>(smem + CHUNK * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int))); int* s_out_idxs = reinterpret_cast<int*>(s_out_vals + num_warps * K); // load chunk for (int i = tid; i < CHUNK; i += blockDim.x) { if (i < chunk_n) { s_vals[i] = row_vals[i]; s_idxs[i] = row_idxs[i]; } else { s_vals[i] = -FLT_MAX; s_idxs[i] = -1; } } __syncthreads(); // warp select top-K from CHUNK / num_warps candidates int warp_chunk = CHUNK / num_warps; float* my_vals = s_vals + warp_id * warp_chunk; int* my_idxs = s_idxs + warp_id * warp_chunk; for (int iter = 0; iter < K; iter++) { float local_max = -FLT_MAX; int local_pos = -1; for (int i = lane; i < warp_chunk; i += 32) { if (my_vals[i] > local_max) { local_max = my_vals[i]; local_pos = i; } } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { float other_max = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFFu, local_max, offset); int other_pos = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFFu, local_pos, offset); if (other_max > local_max) { local_max = other_max; local_pos = other_pos; } } int winner_pos = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFFu, local_pos, 0); float winner_val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFFu, local_max, 0); int winner_idx = my_idxs[winner_pos]; if (lane == 0) { s_out_vals[warp_id * K + iter] = winner_val; s_out_idxs[warp_id * K + iter] = winner_idx; } if (lane == (winner_pos & 31)) { my_vals[winner_pos] = -FLT_MAX; } __syncwarp(); } __syncthreads(); // block sort int block_cand = num_warps * K; for (int kk = 2; kk <= block_cand; kk <<= 1) { for (int j = kk >> 1; j > 0; j >>= 1) { for (int i = tid; i < block_cand; i += blockDim.x) { int ixj = i ^ j; if (ixj > i) { if ((i & kk) == 0) { if (s_out_vals[i] < s_out_vals[ixj]) { float tv = s_out_vals[i]; s_out_vals[i] = s_out_vals[ixj]; s_out_vals[ixj] = tv; int ti = s_out_idxs[i]; s_out_idxs[i] = s_out_idxs[ixj]; s_out_idxs[ixj] = ti; } } else { if (s_out_vals[i] > s_out_vals[ixj]) { float tv = s_out_vals[i]; s_out_vals[i] = s_out_vals[ixj]; s_out_vals[ixj] = tv; int ti = s_out_idxs[i]; s_out_idxs[i] = s_out_idxs[ixj]; s_out_idxs[ixj] = ti; } } } } __syncthreads(); } } if (tid < K) { int out_pos = (row * gridDim.x + block_in_row) * K + tid; out_vals[out_pos] = s_out_vals[tid]; out_idxs[out_pos] = s_out_idxs[tid]; } } ``` In the C++ wrapper: ```cpp int num_candidates = num_blocks_per_row * k; auto tmp2_vals = torch::empty({batch, num_candidates, k}, x.options()); auto tmp2_idxs = torch::empty({batch, num_candidates, k}, torch::dtype(torch::kInt32).device(x.device())); const int CHUNK = 1024; int threads_reduce = 256; int num_warps_reduce = threads_reduce >> 5; size_t smem_reduce = CHUNK * sizeof(float) + CHUNK * sizeof(int) + num_warps_reduce * k * sizeof(float) + num_warps_reduce * k * sizeof(int); while (num_candidates > k) { int blocks_per_row = (num_candidates + CHUNK - 1) / CHUNK; dim3 grid(blocks_per_row, batch); switch (k) { case 8: topk_reduce<8, CHUNK><<<grid, threads_reduce, smem_reduce>>>(...); break; ... } // Swap input/output buffers std::swap(tmp_vals, tmp2_vals); std::swap(tmp_idxs, tmp2_idxs); num_candidates = blocks_per_row * k; } // Final output is in tmp_vals // Copy to out_vals, out_idxs ``` Wait, this requires ping-ponging between two temp buffers. That's fine. For num_candidates=16384, k=64: - Iteration 1: blocks_per_row = 16. num_candidates becomes 1024. - Iteration 2: blocks_per_row = 1. num_candidates becomes 64. Done.  """Custom CUDA top-k kernel optimized for Blackwell SM120.
 
-Two-pass design using bitonic-sort in shared memory (no warp-select shuffles).
-  Pass 1 – many blocks per row sort their chunk in warp SMEM, then block-sort
-           the warp top-k outputs and write to a temporary buffer.
-  Pass 2 – one block per row streams the block results in 1K-element tiles,
-           bitonic-sorts each tile, and merges the tile top-k into the
-           running top-k.

+           block-bitonic-sort the warp top-k outputs, and write to tmp.
+  Pass 2 – iterative reduction: many blocks each warp-select top-k from
+           a 1K-element chunk + block-sort, repeated until one block per row.

The profiler is showing 0.0us or 0.1us for the kernels, which is clearly wrong. The `cuda_time_total` is deprecated and returning 0. Let me use `device_time_total` instead. But actually, the profiler seems broken for this. Let me try a different approach: use `torch.cuda.Event` timing around the Python forward call. I already did this and got ~143us for the old version, and ~83us for the current version. Wait, the benchmark shows 0.083ms for shape 0, which is 83us. That's better than the original 152us, but still slower than torch.topk's 52us. Let me think about this more carefully. Maybe the overhead is not in the kernels themselves, but in the C++ wrapper function. Every time the Python model's forward is called, it calls the C++ function `topk_forward_cuda`, which: 1. Computes `num_blocks_per_row` 2. Allocates 4 temp buffers with `torch::empty` 3. Launches pass 1 kernel 4. Launches pass 2 reduction kernels 5. Launches copy_output kernel The temp buffer allocation with `torch::empty` on CUDA might be taking time! Even though `torch::empty` doesn't zero the memory, it still needs to allocate from PyTorch's CUDA memory pool. For small buffers (like 64KB), PyTorch's allocator should be fast. But if the allocation requires a CUDA API call or synchronization, it could add microseconds. Actually, `torch::empty` in C++ is the same as `torch.empty` in Python. For CUDA tensors, it allocates from the cached allocator. For small sizes, it's very fast. But there might still be some overhead. What if I pre-allocate the temp buffers in the Python Model and pass them to the C++ function? This would eliminate allocation overhead. In Python: ```python class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, batch, n, k): ... if k > 1: num_blocks = max(1, 256 // batch) max_candidates = num_blocks * k self.register_buffer("_tmp_a_vals", torch.empty(batch, max_candidates, dtype=torch.float32)) self.register_buffer("_tmp_a_idxs", torch.empty(batch, max_candidates, dtype=torch.int32)) self.register_buffer("_tmp_b_vals", torch.empty(batch, max_candidates, dtype=torch.float32)) self.register_buffer("_tmp_b_idxs", torch.empty(batch, max_candidates, dtype=torch.int32)) def forward(self, x): return _topk_cuda.topk_forward_cuda(x, self.k, self._tmp_a_vals, self._tmp_a_idxs, self._tmp_b_vals, self._tmp_b_idxs) ``` And in C++: ```cpp std::vector<torch::Tensor> topk_forward_cuda(torch::Tensor x, int k, torch::Tensor tmp_a_vals, torch::Tensor tmp_a_idxs, torch::Tensor tmp_b_vals, torch::Tensor tmp_b_idxs) { // ... no allocations, just use the passed buffers } ``` This would save allocation time on every forward call. But wait, `register_buffer` in Python means the buffers are part of the model state. They get moved to CUDA when `.cuda()` is called. And they're persistent across forward calls. Let me try this! Actually, I also need to pass `num_blocks_per_row` or compute it in Python. In Python: ```python class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, batch, n, k): ... self.num_blocks_per_row = max(1, 256 // batch) if k > 1: max_candidates = self.num_blocks_per_row * k self.register_buffer("_tmp_a_vals", torch.empty(batch, max_candidates, dtype=torch.float32)) ... def forward(self, x): if self.k == 1: return _topk_cuda.topk_forward_cuda(x, self.k) return _topk_cuda.topk_forward_cuda_2pass(x, self.k, self.num_blocks_per_row, self._tmp_a_vals, ...) ``` And in C++: ```cpp std::vector<torch::Tensor> topk_forward_cuda_2pass(torch::Tensor x, int k, int num_blocks_per_row, torch::Tensor tmp_a_vals, torch::Tensor tmp_a_idxs, torch::Tensor tmp_b_vals, torch::Tensor tmp_b_idxs) { // ... } ``` Let me implement this. It should reduce the overhead significantly. Also, I realize that the benchmark does 50 iterations, and the median time includes all the forward pass overhead. Reducing overhead is crucial. Let me rewrite solution.py with pre-allocated buffers. Actually, one more optimization: instead of iterative reduction in pass 2 with multiple kernel launches, I can use a single kernel that does everything if I launch enough blocks. For batch=1, num_candidates=16384: - Launch 16 blocks in pass 2. - Each block processes 1024 candidates and produces 64. - But then I need another launch to merge the 16 * 64 = 1024 candidates. Wait, what if I launch 64 blocks? Each block processes 256 candidates and produces 64. - 64 blocks on 64 SMs: ~0.4us. - But then I have 64 * 64 = 4096 candidates left. Need another iteration. The number of iterations is log(CHUNK) / log(K). For K=64 and CHUNK=1024: 16384 -> 1024 -> 64. 2 iterations. What if CHUNK = 16384? Then 1 iteration! But shared memory for 16384 elements = 128KB. Too much. What if CHUNK = 4096? Shared memory = 32KB + 2KB = 34KB (under 48KB). 16384 / 4096 = 4 blocks. 1 iteration: 4 blocks -> 256 candidates. Need another iteration. Still 2 iterations. What if I use global memory for the tile instead of shared memory? Then I can process larger chunks. Actually, for pass 2, I can process the entire candidate array in one kernel with multiple blocks, and use a final block to merge. But that requires global memory atomics or a second kernel. So 2 iterations is fine. The key is to minimize overhead. Let me implement the pre-allocated buffer version and see if it helps.
